The Third Proof: God's Power Encompasses All Acts
Razi's third proof that human power is superfluous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third proof that God's power covers all possibles, making human power superfluous, by enumerating and eliminating logical possibilities for an act's occurrence.

Core Idea
God's power covers all acts; human power would create impossible conflict.

Razi continues his arguments against human agency by presenting a third proof. This proof builds on the nature of God's power: if God's essence necessarily entails power over all possibles, then every possible act must be within God's power. From this, Razi enumerates the only ways an act could occur and eliminates each, leaving the conclusion that only God creates.

Guided Path
1
Appreciate the starting point
Read the opening about God's essence entailing power over all possibles, either essentially or through a meaning.
This establishes that God's power relates equally to all acts.
2
Understand the enumeration
Identify the three possibilities Razi lists for an act that is within power: by both powers, by neither, or by one without the other.
These are the only logical possibilities.
3
Recognize the invalidation
Note that Razi claims all three are invalid, referencing a previous proof from tawhid. Consider why two powers cannot combine on one act.
The tawhid proof presumably shows independence of powers.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The only remaining possibility is that the servant has no power of origination; only God creates.
Human agency is denied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : على أن العبد غير موجد لأفعال نفسه : هو أن ذاته سبحانه وتعالى مستلزمة للقادرية. إما لنفس ذاته عند من يقول : إنه تعالى قادر لذاته، أو بواسطة كونها مستلزمة لمعنى وذلك المعنى يستلزم القادرية - عند من يقول : إنه تعالى قادر لمعنى - وعلى التقديرين فإن نسبة ذاته إلى جميع الممكنات على السوية. فيلزم أن يكون الله تعالى قادرا على جميع الممكنات وأن يكون تعالى قادرا على المقدورات، وعلى مقدورات العباد جميع. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : ذلك المقدور إما أن يقع بمجموع القدرتين - أعني قدرة الله وقدرة العبد - وإما أن لا يقع بواحدة منهما، وإما أن يقع بإحدى القدرتين دون الأخرى. وهذه الأقسام الثلاثة باطلة، بعين الدليل الذي ذكرناه في مسألة التوحيد، فوجب أن لا يكون العبد قادرًا على الإيجاد والتكوين.
The third proof that the servant is not the creator of his own actions is that His essence, glory be to Him, is necessary for power-ability. Either for His own essence, according to the one who says that He is powerful by His essence, or by virtue of His essence necessitating a meaning and that meaning necessitates power-ability – according to the one who says that He is powerful by a meaning. And on both estimates, the relation of His essence to all possibles is equal. So it follows that God is powerful over all possibles and that He is powerful over the acts that are power-able, and over all the acts of servants. If this is established, we say: that act that is within power either occurs by the combination of the two powers – that is, God’s power and the servant’s power – or it does not occur by either of them, or it occurs by one of the two powers without the other. And these three divisions are invalid, by the very proof that we mentioned in the issue of tawhid, so it must be that the servant is not powerful over bringing about and creating.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that humans do not create their own actions. The proof begins with an assertion about God's essence: it necessarily entails power (either essentially or through a meaning). In either case, God's essence relates equally to all possible things. Therefore, God is powerful over all possibles, including the acts of servants. The argument then considers a particular act that is within power: it can only be attributed to one of three possibilities: (1) it occurs by the combined power of God and the servant, (2) it does not occur by either power, or (3) it occurs by one power alone. Razi claims all three are invalid, referencing a previous proof from the issue of tawhid (divine unity) which presumably shows that two independent powers cannot produce one act, and that an act cannot occur without a power. Therefore, the only remaining possibility is that the servant has no power of origination or creation – only God creates. The structure is a classic kalam argument: enumerate all logical possibilities and eliminate each, leaving the desired conclusion.

التوحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that human power can independently create actions alongside God's power, or that God's power is limited.
Correction: This proof shows logical impossibility: two powers cannot produce one act, and God's power covers all possibles, leaving no room for independent human creation.

Checkpoint

B) Three
C) Four

Key Takeaway
Razi's third proof shows logical impossibility of two independent powers producing the same act, concluding that only God creates.

?

?
When you sin, do you typically blame your own weakness or your free choice? If God's power alone creates all acts, what does that imply about how you should view your sin and your need for divine help to avoid it?

The Fourth Proof: Conflict of Divine and Human Wills
Razi's fourth proof shows that if human power could create, conflict with God's will leads to absurdities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fourth proof that human power cannot create actions, based on the logical impossibility of conflicting divine and human wills.

Core Idea
Human power cannot create actions because that would lead to absurd conflicts with God's will.

This passage presents a powerful logical argument from Imam al-Razi. He imagines a scenario where God wills one thing and a human wills the opposite. By examining all possible outcomes, he shows that if humans had creative power, an absurdity would follow. This proof is a key part of the Ash'ari case for occasionalism.

Guided Path
1
Setting up the hypothetical
Imagine a situation where God wants to still a body, and a servant wants to move it. If the servant had creative power, what must happen?
This is a thought experiment.
2
Three logical possibilities
List the three possible outcomes: both occur, neither occurs, only God's will occurs. Razi argues each is impossible.
The first two are straightforward contradictions.
3
The equal power objection
Razi addresses the objection that God's power is greater because it is infinite. He argues that for a single indivisible act, power cannot be graded.
This is the key subtle point.
4
Conclusion
Since all possibilities are absurd, the premise that the servant can create is false. Only God creates actions.
This reaffirms the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو كانت قدرة العبد صالحة للإيجاد فإذا أراد الله تعالى تسكين جسم، وقدرنا أن العبد أراد تحريكه. فإما أن يقع المرادان وهو محال أو لا يقع واحد منهما. وهو أيضا محال - على ما بيناه في مسألة التوحيد . لأنه خروج عن النقيضين. أو يقع مراد الله تعالى دون مراد العبد وهو أيضا محال. لأن وقوع أحدهما ليس أولى من وقوع الآخر، لأن الله تعالى وإن كان قادرا على ما لا نهاية له، والعبد ليس كذلك . إلا أن ذلك لا يوجب التفاوت بين قدرة الله تعالى وقدرة العبد، في هذه الصورة؛ لأن الحركة الواحدة والسكون الواحد ماهية غير قابلة للقسمة والتفاوت بوجه من الوجوه. وإذا كان المقدور غير قابل للتفاوت فيمتنع أن تكون قدرة الله على إيجاد هذه الحركة أقوى من قدرة العبد على إيجاد السكون بل الله تعالى قادر على سائر المقدورات والعبد غير قادر عليها، لكن ذلك التفاوت لم تكن القدرة على مثل هذا المقدور قابلة للتفاوت لا يوجب التفاوت في القدرة على تلك الحركة، والقدرة على هذا السكون. وإذا ثبت أن قدرة الله تعالى على إيجاد هذه الحركة المعينة لا يمكن أن تكون أقوى من قدرة العبد على إيجاد هذا السكون استحال أن يقال : إن مقدور الله تعالى أولى بالوقوع من مقدور العبد وثبت أن التفاوت الحاصل بسبب أن قدرة الله تعالى متعلقة بما لا نهاية له وقدرة العبد متعلقة بالمتناهي، لا يقدح في ذلك التساوي. فثبت أن تكون قدرة العبد صالحة للإيجاد يلزم أحد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة وثبت أن بتقدير أن كل واحد منها باطل، فلزم القطع بأن قدرة العبد غير صالحة للإيجاد.
The fourth proof: If the servant’s power were valid for bringing into existence, then if God wanted to still a body, and we suppose that the servant wanted to move it, then either both intended things occur – which is impossible – or neither occurs – which is also impossible, as we explained in the issue of tawhid, because it is an exit from the two contradictories – or God’s intended thing occurs without the servant’s intended thing, which is also impossible because the occurrence of one is not more likely than the occurrence of the other, for even though God is powerful over that which has no end and the servant is not, that does not entail disparity between God’s power and the servant’s power in this particular case, because the single motion and the single stillness are an essence not susceptible to division and disparity in any way. And if that which is within power is not susceptible to disparity, then it is impossible for God’s power to bring about this motion to be stronger than the servant’s power to bring about stillness; rather, God is powerful over other acts but the servant is not powerful over them, but that disparity – since the power over such an act is not susceptible to disparity – does not entail disparity in the power over that motion and the power over this stillness. And if it is established that God’s power to bring about this particular motion cannot be stronger than the servant’s power to bring about this stillness, it becomes impossible to say that God’s act within power is more likely to occur than the servant’s act within power; and it is established that the disparity arising because God’s power is related to the infinite and the servant’s power is related to the finite does not affect that equality. So it is established that if the servant’s power were valid for bringing into existence, one of these three divisions would follow, and it is established that each one of them is invalid, so it must be conclusively judged that the servant’s power is not valid for bringing into existence.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof that the servant cannot create his actions. It is a hypothetical argument: suppose the servant's power could truly originate actions. Consider a scenario where God wills to still a body, and the servant wills to move it. There are three logical possibilities: (1) both occur – impossible because a body cannot be both still and moving simultaneously. (2) Neither occurs – impossible because that would be an exit from the two contradictories (a body must be either still or moving). (3) Only God's will occurs – also impossible because there is no reason for one to occur over the other. Razi anticipates the objection that God's power is greater because He has power over infinite things while the servant has finite power. He responds that this does not create disparity in this specific case, because a single motion or stillness is an indivisible essence; one cannot be 'more' powerful than another in bringing about an indivisible act. The power to cause a particular motion or stillness is not quantifiable. Therefore, the supposed inequality in scope (infinite vs. finite) does not affect the equality in this specific instance. Thus, the third possibility (God's will alone occurring) is not inherently more likely than the servant's will alone occurring, leading to a parity that cannot be resolved. Since all three possibilities are absurd, the premise that the servant has creative power is false. The argument relies on the principle of indifferent causation: unless one power is inherently stronger, there is no reason for one effect over the other. Razi uses this to reaffirm that only God can create actions.

Key Terms

الإيجاد
التسكين
التحريك
الماهية
التفاوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's infinite power always makes His will more likely to occur than a human's will, even in a specific conflict.
Correction: Razi argues that for an indivisible act like a single motion or stillness, power cannot be graded; thus, God's power is not stronger in that particular case.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that in the conflict scenario, God's will is not more likely to occur than the servant's will?

B) Because the act of motion or stillness is indivisible and cannot be graded
C) Because the servant's power is stronger in this case
D) Because God does not intervene in such cases

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof demonstrates that human power cannot create actions because it would lead to logical absurdities in a conflict with God's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your will clashes with what you know Allah wants, do you truly believe your own power is incapable of creating any outcome independent of His decree? How does Razi's proof expose the illusion that you can 'make things happen' on your own?
?
Think of a moment you felt pride in your achievement. Does Razi's argument force you to reconsider the source of that success? If your power cannot create even a single motion, what does that mean for your sense of self-sufficiency?
?
Razi claims that for a single act, God's power is not stronger than yours. Does this truth humble you or scare you? Are you willing to surrender the illusion that your will can ever compete with His? What would change in your daily life if you truly lived by this?

The Unbeliever's Intention and Disbelief
Razi begins his fifth proof based on the unbeliever's intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fifth proof from the perspective of the unbeliever's intention and how it demonstrates that actions are not created by the servant.

Core Idea
The unbeliever intends truth, yet disbelief occurs, proving actions are not his creation.

Razi presents his fifth proof in a concise logical form. He starts from a basic premise: voluntary actions only happen when accompanied by intention and choice. He then applies this to the unbeliever, who only intends knowledge and truth, not ignorance. Logically, if the unbeliever were the creator of his actions, only truth would result. But we observe disbelief. Therefore, the action must be from another agent—Allah. This lesson examines that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Read the first part of the passage: 'the voluntary action only occurs from him if it is attached to intention and choice.' This is the foundation of the proof.
Voluntary actions are defined by intention and choice.
2
Apply to the unbeliever
Notice what Razi says: 'the unbeliever does not intend nor choose to bring about ignorance, rather he intends only knowledge and truth.'
The unbeliever's intention is purely for truth.
3
Derive the necessary conclusion
If the servant were the creator of his own actions, then his actions would align with his intention. Since he intends only truth, only truth would occur.
The logical consequence of the premise.
4
Observe the reality
The passage implies that the unbeliever actually produces disbelief (ignorance/falsehood), which contradicts his intention. This contradiction shows that the servant is not the creator of his actions.
The observed outcome contradicts the intention.
5
Recognize the theological implication
The proof leads to the conclusion that Allah is the creator of all actions, including disbelief, but without forcing the servant (since the servant's intention is still considered).
This supports the Ash'ari position on kasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : أن الفعل الاختياري لا يقع منه إلا ما تعلق به القصد والاختيار والكافر لا يقصد ولا يختار إحداث الجهل، بل لا يقصد إلا العلم والصدق، فكان يجب أن لا يحصل له إلا العلم والصدق ولما قصد العلم وحصل
The fifth proof: that the voluntary action only occurs from him if it is attached to intention and choice, and the unbeliever does not intend nor choose to bring about ignorance, rather he intends only knowledge and truth. So it would be necessary that nothing but knowledge and truth be obtained for him. And when he intended knowledge and [it] occurred
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage starts the fifth proof that the servant is not the creator of his own actions. It is incomplete but presents the core idea: voluntary actions occur only when accompanied by intention and choice. The unbeliever (kaafir) does not intend or choose to produce ignorance (jahl); rather, he only intends knowledge and truth. Therefore, if the servant were the creator of his own actions, only knowledge and truth would come from him, because he only intends those. Yet the unbeliever does produce ignorance and falsehood (such as disbelief). The argument implies that since the result (ignorance) contradicts the intention, the action cannot be created by the servant himself—it must be created by God. The fragment at the end 'and when he intended knowledge and it occurred' may be part of a conditional or contrast, but the text breaks off. The proof relies on the principle that the creator of an act must intend that act; if the act is contrary to intention, the agent is not the creator.

The Fourth Proof: Conflicting Wills and Impossibility
Razi enumerates three impossible outcomes if servant's power were creative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fourth proof that the servant's power cannot create acts, and why the three possible outcomes of conflicting divine and human wills are all impossible.

Core Idea
If both God and servant could create, contradictory acts would lead to logical absurdities.

Razi now presents a powerful logical argument against the idea that human power can bring about existence. He sets up a direct conflict between divine will and human will, and shows that every possible outcome leads to contradiction. This proof forces us to see that the servant's power cannot be creative.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: a scenario, three possibilities, each argued impossible, and a conclusion.
This proof assumes the servant's power could bring acts into existence; Razi will disprove that assumption.
2
Identify the scenario
God wills stillness in a body; the servant wills motion. These are contradictory acts. What are the possible outcomes?
Motion and stillness cannot coexist in the same body at the same time.
3
Understand the three possibilities
Razi lists three: both occur, neither occurs, or only God's intended occurs. He argues each is impossible.
The third possibility is the most subtle and relies on the indivisibility of the act.
4
Grasp why each is impossible
Both: impossible because they are opposites. Neither: impossible because one must occur (law of excluded middle). Only God's: impossible because there is no reason for one to prevail over the other if power is equal in this specific act.
Razi argues that even though God's power is infinite overall, in this specific indivisible act there is no disparity.
5
See the conclusion
Since all three outcomes are impossible, the original assumption that the servant's power is creative must be false.
Thus the servant's power does not bring acts into existence; it only acquires or earns them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو كانت قدرة العبد صالحة للإيجاد فإذا أراد الله تعالى تسكين جسم، وقدرنا أن العبد أراد تحريكه. فإما أن يقع المرادان وهو محال أو لا يقع واحد منهما. وهو أيضا محال - على ما بيناه في مسألة التوحيد . لأنه خروج عن النقيضين. أو يقع مراد الله تعالى دون مراد العبد وهو أيضا محال. لأن وقوع أحدهما ليس أولى من وقوع الآخر، لأن الله تعالى وإن كان قادرا على ما لا نهاية له، والعبد ليس كذلك . إلا أن ذلك لا يوجب التفاوت بين قدرة الله تعالى وقدرة العبد، في هذه الصورة؛ لأن الحركة الواحدة والسكون الواحد ماهية غير قابلة للقسمة والتفاوت بوجه من الوجوه. وإذا كان المقدور غير قابل للتفاوت فيمتنع أن تكون قدرة الله على إيجاد هذه الحركة أقوى من قدرة العبد على إيجاد السكون بل الله تعالى قادر على سائر المقدورات والعبد غير قادر عليها، لكن ذلك التفاوت لم تكن القدرة على مثل هذا المقدور قابلة للتفاوت لا يوجب التفاوت في القدرة على تلك الحركة، والقدرة على هذا السكون. وإذا ثبت أن قدرة الله تعالى على إيجاد هذه الحركة المعينة لا يمكن أن تكون أقوى من قدرة العبد على إيجاد هذا السكون استحال أن يقال : إن مقدور الله تعالى أولى بالوقوع من مقدور العبد وثبت أن التفاوت الحاصل بسبب أن قدرة الله تعالى متعلقة بما لا نهاية له وقدرة العبد متعلقة بالمتناهي، لا يقدح في ذلك التساوي. فثبت أن تكون قدرة العبد صالحة للإيجاد يلزم أحد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة وثبت أن بتقدير أن كل واحد منها باطل، فلزم القطع بأن قدرة العبد غير صالحة للإيجاد.
The fourth proof: If the power of the servant were valid for bringing into existence, then if God Almighty intended to cause stillness in a body, and we assume that the servant intended to cause its motion, then either both intended objects would occur – which is impossible – or neither of them would occur – which is also impossible, as we have explained in the issue of tawḥīd, because it would be a departure from the two contradictories – or God's intended object would occur without the servant's intended object – which is also impossible, because the occurrence of one of them is not more appropriate than the occurrence of the other, for although God Almighty is powerful over what has no end and the servant is not, nevertheless that does not necessitate a disparity between God's power and the servant's power in this particular case, because a single motion and a single stillness are a quiddity not subject to division or disparity in any respect. And if the object of power is not subject to disparity, then it is impossible that God's power to bring about this motion be stronger than the servant's power to bring about this stillness. Rather, God Almighty is powerful over other objects of power and the servant is not powerful over them, but that disparity – since power over such an object of power is not subject to disparity – does not necessitate disparity in the power over that motion and the power over this stillness. And when it is established that God's power to bring about this specific motion cannot be stronger than the servant's power to bring about this stillness, it becomes impossible to say that God's object of power is more deserving of occurrence than the servant's object of power. And it is established that the disparity resulting from the fact that God's power is related to what is infinite and the servant's power is related to what is finite does not affect this equality. Thus it is established that if the servant's power were valid for bringing into existence, one of these three divisions would necessarily follow, and it is established that, assuming each one of them is false, it necessarily follows decisively that the servant's power is not valid for bringing into existence.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the fourth proof) against the idea that the servant's power can create acts. The author sets up a scenario where God wills stillness in a body and the servant wills motion. He then enumerates three possibilities: both occur (impossible because they are contradictory), neither occurs (impossible because that would be a departure from the two contradictories, meaning one must occur), or only God's will occurs (also impossible because there is no reason for one to prevail over the other). He argues that even though God has infinite power and the servant finite, that does not create a disparity in this specific case because the motion and stillness are simple, indivisible quiddities not subject to degrees. Therefore, God's power cannot be stronger in this specific act, so preference cannot be justified. Thus, the only way to avoid these impossible outcomes is to deny that the servant's power is creative. The proof concludes that the servant's power does not bring about existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because God's power is infinite, His will always prevails over the servant's will in every specific act.
Correction: The proof shows that in an indivisible act like a single motion or stillness, power cannot be stronger or weaker, so there is no basis for preference. God's overall infinite power does not affect equality in this specific case.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, what is the third impossible scenario if both God and servant have creative power?
A) Both intended objects occur.
B) Neither occurs.
C) Only God's intended object occurs.
D) The servant's intended object occurs.

Key Takeaway
The servant's power cannot be creative because if it were, conflicting wills would lead to logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own power cannot truly create any act, how does that change your view of your achievements and failures? Will you stop attributing your successes to your own ability and instead see them as God's creation?
?
This proof shows that in the simplest act, your power is not creative. Are you still holding onto a sense of self-sufficiency in your daily actions? How can you live with constant awareness that every movement is from Allah?
?
The proof eliminates the possibility of your will competing with God's. Does your heart truly accept that nothing happens without His will, or do you still nurse a secret belief that you can override His decree by your own power? Repent of that thought.

The Fifth Proof: Ignorance and Infinite Regress
Razi gives the fifth proof about the origin of ignorance in the unbeliever.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first ignorance of the unbeliever must be created by God, based on the argument against infinite regress.

Core Idea
An infinite chain of ignorances is impossible; only God can create the first ignorance.

In this proof, Razi addresses the problem of the unbeliever's ignorance. How can a person who desires knowledge end up with ignorance? The answer lies in a chain that cannot go back forever. Let's follow the argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the tight logical structure.
2
Identify the core claim
The fifth proof states that voluntary acts require intention. The unbeliever intends knowledge, not ignorance. So ignorance must come from elsewhere.
This is the premise.
3
Examine the objection and response
The objection says the unbeliever intended ignorance because he thought it was knowledge. Razi's response shows this leads to an infinite regress.
4
Understand the conclusion
The regress is impossible, so there must be a first ignorance not chosen by man, hence created by God.
This establishes divine creation of that ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : أن الفعل الاختياري لا يقع منه إلا ما تعلق به القصد والاختيار والكافر لا يقصد ولا يختار إحداث الجهل، بل لا يقصد إلا العلم والصدق، فكان يجب أن لا يحصل له إلا العلم والصدق ولما قصد العلم وحصل له الجهل، علمنا أن وقوعه ليس بإيقاعه بل بإيقاع غيره. فإن قيل : إنما حصل ذلك الجهل لأنه ظن أنه علم، فلا جرم قصد إيقاعه. قلنا : فهو إنما اختار الجهل بسبب أنه كان ذلك الجهل المتقدم حاصلا له فإن كان القول فيه كما في الأول لزم أن يكون كل جهل مسبوقا بجهل آخر، لا إلى بداية وذلك محال، فإذن لابد من انتهاء هذه الجهالات إلى جهل أول غير مسبوق بجهل آخر وذلك الجهل الأول يستحيل أن يكون بسبب أن الإنسان اختاره لأن الإنسان لا يختار ذلك البتة . فثبت أن ذلك الجهل الأول إنما حصل بإيجاد الله تعالى وتخليقه وسائر الجهالات تفرعت عليه فكان الكل مستندا بالحقيقة إلى تخليق الله تعالى وتكوينه.
The fifth proof: A voluntary act only occurs from one when intent and choice are attached to it. The unbeliever does not intend nor choose to produce ignorance; rather, he only intends knowledge and truth. So it should be that only knowledge and truth occur from him. But when he intended knowledge and ignorance occurred to him, we know that its occurrence is not by his bringing about, but by the bringing about of another. If it is said: 'That ignorance occurred to him only because he thought it was knowledge, so he intended to bring it about.' We say: He only chose ignorance because that previous ignorance was already present in him. If the same reasoning is applied to that [previous ignorance], then every ignorance must be preceded by another ignorance, ad infinitum, and that is impossible. Therefore, these ignorances must terminate at a first ignorance not preceded by any other ignorance. That first ignorance cannot possibly be due to the human's choice, because the human never chooses that at all. Thus it is established that that first ignorance only occurs by God's bringing into existence and His creation, and all the other ignorances branched off from it, so all of them are in reality dependent on God's creation and bringing into existence.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This is the fifth proof, focusing on the unbeliever's ignorance. The argument: a voluntary act requires intention and choice. The unbeliever only intends knowledge and truth, not ignorance. Yet ignorance occurs. Therefore, it cannot be by his own doing. An objection is raised: perhaps he intended ignorance because he thought it was knowledge. The answer: that only pushes the problem back, because that ignorance was itself caused by a previous ignorance, leading to an infinite regress. That regress is impossible. So there must be a first ignorance not chosen by the human, which must be created by God. Hence all ignorance ultimately comes from God.

Key Terms
فعل اختياري
قصد
اختيار
جهل
علم
إيقاع
تخليق
تكوين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The unbeliever's ignorance is entirely his own fault because he freely chooses it.
Correction: The passage shows that this leads to an infinite regress, so the first ignorance must be created by God, not chosen.

Checkpoint
Why must the first ignorance of the unbeliever be created by God?
A) Because the unbeliever intended it.
B) Because an infinite regress of ignorances is impossible.
C) Because God commands belief.
D) Because the servant has no power at all.

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress argument shows that the first ignorance of the unbeliever must be directly created by God, not chosen by the human.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what specific areas of your faith are you choosing to remain ignorant because the truth would be uncomfortable? How does Razi's argument that even your first ignorance is from God challenge you to actively seek knowledge this week? What one step will you take to replace that ignorance with truth starting today?
?
Consider a time you stubbornly held onto a false belief. Can you trace that belief back to a first ignorance you never questioned? How does knowing that the origin of that ignorance is ultimately from God (not your own choice) change your attitude toward that past mistake? Will you now seek repentance and knowledge?
?
If all human ignorance ultimately traces back to God's creation, how should that affect your interactions with people who disagree with you on matters of faith? Does it make you more humble and compassionate, or does it increase your sense of responsibility to call them to the truth? What will you actually do this week to embody that response?

Razi's First Face: Turning the Tables on the Mu'tazila
Razi answers the Mu'tazila's objection with the first of six faces, then begins the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses God's foreknowledge to argue that the Mu'tazila's own position leads to the very doctrine they criticize: imposing an impossible obligation.

Core Idea
Razi turns the Mu'tazila's objection back on them using divine knowledge.

The Mu'tazila argue that without independent human power, God's commands and punishments become unjust. Razi responds with a six-point rebuttal. The first point turns the tables: God's eternal knowledge that someone will not believe makes commanding belief a command of the impossible. Thus the Mu'tazila themselves must accept taklif ma la yutaq.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila's objection
Read the opening sentence where the Mu'tazila claim that without independent power (istiqlal) over actions, commands, prohibitions, praise, blame, reward, and punishment would be meaningless.
This sets up the problem they think Ash'aris face.
2
Examine Razi's first face
Notice how Razi argues that the Mu'tazila themselves face a similar problem: God's knowledge of future disbelief is incompatible with commanding faith.
The argument uses the example of Abu Lahab.
3
Analyze the example of Abu Lahab
God knew from eternity that Abu Lahab would never believe, yet commanded him to believe. This is a command to combine two contradictories (belief and knowledge of non-belief).
This makes the command impossible to fulfill, i.e., 'taklif ma la yutaq'.
4
Grasp the consequence
Razi concludes that the Mu'tazila are forced to accept the very doctrine they reject, unless they deny that God knows future contingents, which most Mu'tazila consider disbelief.
This shows the strength of Razi's counter-argument.
5
Note the incomplete second face
The passage begins a second face with a verse citation but cuts off. Observe that the argument would continue, but we focus on the complete first face.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعتزلة، فكلامهم في هذا الباب في غاية الكثرة والبسط، إلا أنه يرجع الكل إلى حرف واحد وهو أنه لولا الاستقلال بالفعل لكان الأمر والنهي والمدح والذم والثواب والعقاب باطلا . والجواب : أن هذا السؤال لازم عليكم أيضا من ستة أوجه : الأول : هو أن العلم بعدم الإيمان ووجود الإيمان متضادان متنافيان لذاتيهما، كما أن الحركة والسكون متنافيان متضادان لذاتيهما. وذلك لأن العلم بعدم الإيمان لا يكون علما، إلا مع عدم الإيمان، وعدم الإيمان ووجوده متنافيان، فوجب القول بأن العلم بعدم الإيمان مناف ومضاد لوجود الإيمان، وكما أن الأمر بالجمع بين الحركة والسكون أمر بإيجاد ما يمتنع وجوده، فكذلك الأمر بالإيمان، مع قيام العلم بعدم الإيمان أمر بالجمع بين الضدين. إذا عرفت ،هذا فنقول : إنه تعالى كان عالما من الأزل إلى الأبد، بأن أبا لهب لا يؤمن ثم إنه كان يأمره بالإيمان، فكان هذا أمرا بالجمع بين النقيضين. وهو محال. فالقول بتكليف ما لا يطاق لازم عليه في مسألة العلم، كما أنه لازم علينا في مسألة خلق الأفعال. ولو أن جملة العقلاء اجتمعوا وأرادوا أن يوردوا على هذا الكلام حرفا ، لم يقدروا عليه. إلا أن يلتزموا مذهب هشام بن الحكم وهو أنه تعالى لا يعلم الأشياء قبل وقوعها، لا بالوجود ولا بالعدم إلا أن أكثر المعتزلة يكفرون من يقول بهذا القول. والله أعلم. الوجه الثاني في الإلزام أنه تعالى قال : وإِنَّ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا سَوَاءٌ عَلَيْهِمْ
As for the Muʿtazila, their speech in this topic is extremely plentiful and extensive, but it all reduces to a single point: that without independent power over the act, commands and prohibitions, praise and blame, reward and punishment would be invalid. The answer: This objection is also necessary against you from six aspects: The first: That knowledge of the non-existence of faith and the existence of faith are mutually exclusive and contradictory in their essences, just as motion and stillness are mutually exclusive and contradictory in their essences. That is because knowledge of the non-existence of faith is only knowledge when accompanied by the non-existence of faith, and the non-existence of faith and its existence are mutually contradictory. Hence it necessarily follows that knowledge of the non-existence of faith is incompatible and contradictory to the existence of faith. And just as commanding the combination of motion and stillness is a command to bring about what is impossible to exist, so too commanding faith while knowledge of the non-existence of faith is present is a command to combine two opposites. Once this is understood, we say: God Almighty knew from eternity to eternity that Abū Lahab would not believe, and then He commanded him to believe. This was a command to combine two contradictories, and that is impossible. Thus the doctrine of imposing an impossible obligation (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq) is necessary against them in the issue of divine knowledge, just as it is necessary against us in the issue of the creation of acts. If all the rational people gathered and tried to produce a single word against this speech, they would not be able to, unless they adopt the doctrine of Hishām ibn al-Ḥakam, which is that God does not know things before they occur, either in existence or non-existence – but most Muʿtazila declare whoever says this a disbeliever. And God knows best. The second aspect of the rebuttal: that God Almighty said: 'Indeed, those who disbelieve, it is all the same to them...'
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's central objection: if God creates all acts, then commands, prohibitions, praise, blame, reward, and punishment become meaningless. The author responds with a six-fold rebuttal, of which the first two (incomplete) are given here. The first face argues that the Mu'tazila themselves face a similar problem: God's knowledge of the future. For example, God knew from eternity that Abu Lahab would not believe, yet commanded him to believe. This is a command to combine two contradictories (belief and knowledge of non-belief), which is impossible. Therefore, the Mu'tazila are forced to accept the doctrine of impositions of the impossible (taklif ma la yutaq) in the realm of divine knowledge, just as they accuse the Ash'aris of doing in the realm of action. The author then notes that the only way out for the Mu'tazila would be to deny that God knows future contingents, a position held by Hisham ibn al-Hakam, but which most Mu'tazila consider disbelief. The second face begins by citing a verse, but it is cut off.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila's objection about impossible obligation applies only to the Ash'ari position on human power.
Correction: Razi shows that the Mu'tazila themselves face the same problem because God's foreknowledge creates an impossible obligation when He commands someone He knows will not comply.

Checkpoint
What does the first face of the answer show against the Mu'tazila?
A) That God cannot command belief.
B) That God's knowledge of disbelief makes commanding belief a command of the impossible.
C) That the servant has independent power.
D) That divine knowledge is not known.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first face shows that the Mu'tazila's objection about impossible obligation backfires because God's foreknowledge creates the same problem for them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does God's eternal knowledge of your choices—including your sins—affect the way you face temptation? Do you ever use 'it was written' as an excuse? After this lesson, what must you acknowledge about your responsibility despite His foreknowledge?
?
Razi uses Abu Lahab as an example of someone whose disbelief was known and inevitable. Is there someone in your life whom you have written off as beyond guidance? How does the fact that God commands even those He knows will not believe challenge your own despair for others?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted that without independent power, commands would be meaningless. But Razi shows that even God's commands can appear 'impossible' if judged by human standards. When you encounter a command of Allah that seems impossible for you to fulfill—like complete patience or perfect sincerity—do you retreat, or do you submit and strive anyway? Why?

The Mu'tazila Objection and Razi's First Refutation: Divine Foreknowledge
Razi presents the Mu'tazila objection and offers the first of six refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila objection against predestination and Razi's refutation using divine foreknowledge, showing that the objection also applies to them.

Core Idea
Divine foreknowledge creates an impossible obligation, just as predestination does.

The Mu'tazila argue that without free will, moral commands are meaningless. Razi responds by turning the tables: divine foreknowledge of disbelief makes belief an impossible command, a problem the Mu'tazila cannot escape. This lesson examines their objection and Razi's first counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the two parts: the Mu'tazila objection and Razi's refutation.
The refutation is the first of six; we only cover the first here.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila claim that without independence in action, commands and moral categories become invalid. Understand their core point.
3
Analyze Razi's analogy
Razi compares the contradiction between knowledge of disbelief and belief to that between motion and rest. See how commanding belief when disbelief is known is like commanding two opposites simultaneously.
The analogy shows that the Mu'tazila face the same 'impossible obligation' problem they attribute to the Ash'aris.
4
Consider the consequence
Razi applies the analogy to God's foreknowledge of Abu Lahab's disbelief. This leads to the conclusion that the Mu'tazila's own view on divine knowledge entails an impossible command, unless they adopt the extreme position of Hisham ibn al-Hakam.
Most Mu'tazila reject that position, so the objection stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعتزلة، فكلامهم في هذا الباب في غاية الكثرة والبسط، إلا أنه يرجع الكل إلى حرف واحد وهو أنه لولا الاستقلال بالفعل لكان الأمر والنهي والمدح والذم والثواب والعقاب باطلا .
والجواب : أن هذا السؤال لازم عليكم أيضا من ستة أوجه :
الأول : هو أن العلم بعدم الإيمان ووجود الإيمان متضادان متنافيان لذاتيهما، كما أن الحركة والسكون متنافيان متضادان لذاتيهما. وذلك لأن العلم بعدم الإيمان لا يكون علما، إلا مع عدم الإيمان، وعدم الإيمان ووجوده متنافيان، فوجب القول بأن العلم بعدم الإيمان مناف ومضاد لوجود الإيمان، وكما أن الأمر بالجمع بين الحركة والسكون أمر بإيجاد ما يمتنع وجوده، فكذلك الأمر بالإيمان، مع قيام العلم بعدم الإيمان أمر بالجمع بين الضدين.
إذا عرفت ،هذا فنقول : إنه تعالى كان عالما من الأزل إلى الأبد، بأن أبا لهب لا يؤمن ثم إنه كان يأمره بالإيمان، فكان هذا أمرا بالجمع بين النقيضين. وهو محال. فالقول بتكليف ما لا يطاق لازم عليه في مسألة العلم، كما أنه لازم علينا في مسألة خلق الأفعال. ولو أن جملة العقلاء اجتمعوا وأرادوا أن يوردوا على هذا الكلام حرفا ، لم يقدروا عليه. إلا أن يلتزموا مذهب هشام بن الحكم وهو أنه تعالى لا يعلم الأشياء قبل وقوعها، لا بالوجود ولا بالعدم إلا أن أكثر المعتزلة يكفرون من يقول بهذا القول. والله أعلم.
As for the Mu'tazila, their words on this topic are extremely numerous and detailed, but all of them reduce to one point: that if there were no independence in action, then command and prohibition, praise and blame, reward and punishment would be invalid. The answer: This objection applies to you as well from six aspects: The first: Knowledge of disbelief and existence of belief are mutually contradictory and opposed in their essences, just as motion and rest are mutually opposed and contradictory in their essences. That is because knowledge of disbelief is only knowledge when there is disbelief, and disbelief and its existence are mutually contradictory. Therefore, it must be said that knowledge of disbelief is contradictory and opposed to the existence of belief. And just as commanding the combination of motion and rest is a command to bring about what is impossible to exist, so likewise commanding belief while knowledge of disbelief is present is a command to combine two opposites. If you understand this, then we say: God Most High knew from eternity to eternity that Abu Lahab would not believe, then He commanded him to believe. This was a command to combine two opposites, which is impossible. So the doctrine of imposing an impossible obligation is incumbent upon them in the issue of [divine] knowledge, just as it is incumbent upon us in the issue of the creation of actions. And if all rational people gathered and tried to raise a single objection against this speech, they would not be able to, unless they adopt the doctrine of Hisham ibn al-Hakam, that God does not know things before they occur, neither as existent nor as non-existent, except that most Mu'tazila declare those who say this to be disbelievers. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila objection and Razi's first refutation. The Mu'tazila argue that if humans are not independent agents (i.e., if God creates all actions), then moral commands, prohibitions, praise, blame, reward, and punishment would be meaningless. Razi responds that this objection also applies to the Mu'tazila themselves, from six angles. The first angle: Divine foreknowledge of a person's disbelief contradicts the possibility of them believing. Razi draws an analogy: knowledge of disbelief requires that disbelief actually occurs, just as motion and rest are mutually exclusive. Therefore, commanding someone to believe when God knows they will not believe is a command to combine two opposites, which is impossible. This shows that the Mu'tazila also face the problem of impossible obligation (تكليف ما لا يطاق) regarding divine foreknowledge. Razi argues that this refutation is unassailable unless one adopts the extreme view of Hisham ibn al-Hakam that God does not know things before they happen, which most Mu'tazila reject as disbelief.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الاستقلال بالفعل
تكليف ما لا يطاق
العلم بالعدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is denying free will entirely, but he is only showing that the Mu'tazila face a similar problem with divine foreknowledge.
Correction: Razi's argument is a dialectical refutation, not a denial of free will per se. He uses the Mu'tazila's own premises against them.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila's main objection that Razi addresses?
A) That God cannot know future events.
B) That without free will, moral responsibility becomes meaningless.
C) That ignorance is impossible.
D) That God commands only what is possible.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first refutation demonstrates that the problem of impossible obligation applies to divine foreknowledge, not only to predestination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God already knows every sin you will commit, does that make you more careless in avoiding sin or more determined to struggle against it? What does your answer reveal about your understanding of responsibility?
?
Abu Lahab was commanded to believe even though God knew he would not. How does that truth confront any excuse you might use to delay obedience or repentance?
?
If God's command is meaningful even when He knows you will fail, what does that say about the weight of His command in your life right now? Will you respond to His command today, or will you wait until you feel 'ready'?

The Second Refutation: God's Report and the Impossibility of Belief
Razi uses Quran 2:6 to argue disbelief is predetermined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second refutation: how God's true report about disbelievers logically prevents them from believing, yet He still commands them to believe.

Core Idea
God's true report cannot be false; thus those He says won't believe cannot believe.

Razi continues his refutations against the Mu'tazila, focusing on a key Quranic verse. In this lesson, we examine his argument that God's own report about the disbelievers logically prevents them from believing, yet God still commands them to believe. This highlights the Ash'ari view of divine decree and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the second refutation once, then read the English translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Notice the verse from Surat al-Baqarah.
2
Identify the logical structure
Razi starts with a Quranic verse, then makes a conditional: if these people were to believe, God's report would become false. Since falsehood from God is impossible, their belief is impossible.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the occurrence of belief from them is impossible, yet God commands them to believe. This highlights the tension between divine command and divine foreknowledge.
Razi uses this to argue against the Mu'tazila position that humans have free will to choose belief.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: How does this argument support the Ash'ari doctrine of predestination? How would a Mu'tazila respond?
This refutation is one of six, each targeting a different aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في الإلزام أنه تعالى قال : وإِنَّ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا سَوَاءٌ عَلَيْهِمْ أَنذَرْتَهُمْ أَمْ لَمْ تُنذِرْهُمْ لَا يُؤْمِنُونَ [البقرة : ٦] . فأولئك الذين أخبر الله عنهم بهذا الخبر، لو آمنوا لانقلب هذا الخبر كذبا والكذب على الله تعالى محال، والمفضي إلى المحال محال، فكان صدور الإيمان عنهم مع ان الله تعالى كان يأمرهم بالإيمان محالا
الوجه الثالث : أنه تعالى كلف أبا لهب بالإيمان، ومن جملة الإيمان تصديق الله تعالى في كل ما أخبر عنه ومما أخبر عنه : أن أبا لهب لا يؤمن فقد صار أبو لهب مكلفا بأن يؤمن بأنه لا يؤمن وهذا تكليف بالجمع بين النقيضين.
The second aspect of the imposition: That God Most High said: 'Indeed, those who disbelieved – it is the same for them whether you warn them or do not warn them – they will not believe' [Al-Baqarah 2:6]. Those people about whom God informed with this report, if they were to believe, this report would become false. And falsehood against God Most High is impossible, and what leads to the impossible is itself impossible. So the occurrence of belief from them while God Most High was commanding them to believe is impossible. The third aspect: That God Most High obligated Abu Lahab to believe, and part of belief is to affirm God Most High in everything He has reported. And among what He reported is that Abu Lahab will not believe. Thus Abu Lahab became obligated to believe that he does not believe, and this is an obligation to combine two opposites.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage contains the second and third of Razi's six refutations against the Mu'tazila. The second refutation uses the Quranic verse from Surat al-Baqarah (2:6) which states that the disbelievers will not believe regardless of warning. Razi argues that if these disbelievers were to believe despite God's report, then God's report would be false. Since lying is impossible for God, and whatever leads to the impossible is itself impossible, therefore the occurrence of belief from them is impossible. Yet God still commands them to believe, which is an impossible obligation. The third refutation focuses on Abu Lahab. God commanded him to believe, and part of belief is to affirm all of God's reports. But God reported that Abu Lahab will not believe. Thus Abu Lahab is obligated to believe that he does not believe, which is a contradiction: he must affirm both that he believes and that he does not believe simultaneously. This is an impossible obligation.

Key Terms
الخبر (report)
الكذب (falsehood/lie)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is denying God's command to believe, but he is highlighting the inconsistency in the Mu'tazila position on divine foreknowledge.
Correction: Razi is not saying God's command is invalid; he is showing that the Mu'tazila cannot reconcile God's foreknowledge with their view of human free will.

Checkpoint

A) God would be pleased.
B) God's report would become false.
C) The verse would be abrogated.
D) The angels would intervene.

Key Takeaway
God's true report of disbelief makes belief from those disbelievers impossible, yet He commands it, challenging the Mu'tazila view of human capability.

The Third Refutation: Abu Lahab and the Contradiction
Razi uses Abu Lahab to show a logical contradiction in divine command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third refutation: how God's command to Abu Lahab to believe in all divine reports leads to a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Obliging Abu Lahab to believe in God's report of his disbelief is commanding the impossible.

Razi's third refutation targets the specific case of Abu Lahab, a figure explicitly mentioned in the Qur'an as doomed to unbelief. In this lesson, we see how God's command to believe includes believing in God's report that Abu Lahab will not believe, creating an impossible obligation. This argument further undermines the Mu'tazila notion of universal ability to believe.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the third refutation and the English translation. Focus on the chain of reasoning.
Abu Lahab is mentioned in Surah Al-Masad.
2
Identify the components of belief
Razi notes that belief includes affirming all that God has reported. So to believe, Abu Lahab must affirm God's report that he will not believe.
This sets up the contradiction.
3
Recognize the contradiction
Abu Lahab is required to believe that he does not believe. This is a logical contradiction: one cannot simultaneously affirm and deny belief.
This is the 'combining of two opposites'.
4
Understand the implication
Since God does not command the impossible, this refutes the Mu'tazila view that all are equally able to believe.
For Razi, this shows that God's decree overrides human ability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Fourth Refutation: The State of Motives
Razi argues that obligation is impossible whether motives are equal or one prevails.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that obligation is impossible whether motives are equal or one prevails, via logical contradiction and impossibility.

Core Idea
Obligation fails both when motives are balanced and when they tip.

This passage from Razi's al-Arba'in tackles a key Mu'tazili claim about obligation. He examines the logical consequences of obligating a person when their motives are exactly balanced or when one motive dominates. The argument uses a careful analysis of contradiction and impossibility. Read on to see how he dismantles both scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two cases of motives
Razi divides the state of motives into two: equality or preponderance. Identify each case and why he says each leads to impossible obligation.
Equality and preponderance are contradictory in the first case.
2
Analyze the equality case
If motives are equal, obligating choosing one over the other (tarjīḥ) combines equality and preponderance, a contradiction.
3
Analyze the preponderance case
If one motive is preponderant, the act is either necessary (rājiḥ) or impossible (marjūḥ), both beyond the agent's capacity.
The preponderant act is necessary, so cannot be caused by choice.
4
Follow the objection and response
Razi considers an objection that the command applies in a future state, then shows the problem recurs.
The objection tries to avoid the immediate contradiction but fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : هو أن توجه التكليف على العبد إما أن يكون حال استواء الداعي إلى الفعل أو الترك، أو حال رجحان أحد الداعيين على الآخر. أما الأول فهو تكليف بما لا يطاق. لأن الاستواء والرجحان متناقضان، فلو كلف حال الاستواء بالترجيح لكان قد وقع التكليف بالجمع بين النقيضين. وأما الثاني فهو أيضا تكليف بما لا يطاق؛ لأنه إن كلف بالراجح، فالراجح واجب الوقوع - على ما بيناه - وما كان واجب الوقوع لنفسه، استحال أن يكون وقوعه بإيقاع موقع منفصل، فكان أمره بإيقاعه أمرا له بما ليس في وسعه وإن كلف بالمرجوح فالمرجوح ممتنع الوقوع فكان هذا أمرا بإيقاع ما يكون ممتنع الوقوع والله أعلم. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه حال استواء الداعيين، يكون مأمورا بالترجيح لا في هذه الحالة التي هي حالة الاستواء، بل في الحالة الثانية منهما. قلنا : إذا توجه عليه هذا التكليف في لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه حال استواء الداعيين، يكون مأمورا حال الاستواء، فإما أن يؤمر حال الاستواء بأن يحصل الترجيح في هذه الحالة التي هي . حال الاستواء، فيكون هذا جمعا بين النقيضين. وإما أن يقال : إنه في هذه الحالة مأمور بأن يوقع الترجيح في الحالة الثانية من هذه الحالة. ونقول : هذا القسم أيضا ينقسم إلى قسمين. وذلك لأنه إما أن يكون المراد منه : أنه حال الاستواء أعلمه أنه إذا جاءت الحالة الثانية، فهو في تلك الحالة الثانية مأمور بإيقاع الترجيح، لكن على هذا التقدير لا تكليف عليه في حال الاستواء، بل مكلفا. أعلمه أنه في الحالة الثانية سيصير وإذا كان كذلك فنحن نقول : عند مجيء الحالة الثانية إما أن يكون الحال حال الاستواء، أو حال الترجيح. ويعود الإشكال المذكور وإما أن يكون المراد منه : أنه
،
في حالة الاستواء مأمور في هذه الحالة، بأن يوقع الرجحان في الحالة الثانية.
The fourth aspect: That the direction of obligation upon the servant is either in a state of equality of the motive towards action or restraint, or in a state of preponderance of one of the two motives over the other. As for the first, it is an impossible obligation. Because equality and preponderance are contradictory, so if one is obligated during equality to bring about preponderance, that would be an obligation to combine two opposites. As for the second, it is also an impossible obligation; because if he is obligated to do the preponderant, the preponderant is necessarily occurring—as we have explained—and that which is necessarily occurring in itself is impossible for its occurrence to be by the causing of a separate agent, so commanding him to bring it about is commanding him to do what is beyond his capacity. And if he is obligated to do the outweighed, the outweighed is impossible of occurrence, so this is a command to bring about what is impossible of occurrence. And God knows best. If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that in the state of equality of the two motives, he is commanded to bring about preponderance not in this state which is the state of equality, but rather in the second state after it? We say: When this obligation is directed upon him... [text unclear] why is it not permissible to say that in the state of equality of the two motives, he is commanded during equality? Then either he is commanded during equality to bring about preponderance in this state which is the state of equality, so this is a combination of two opposites. Or it is said that in this state he is commanded to bring about preponderance in the second state after this state. We say: This division also divides into two parts. That is because either it means that during equality He informed him that when the second state comes, he is in that second state commanded to bring about preponderance. However, on this assumption, there is no obligation upon him in the state of equality, rather he is obligated [only later]. He informed him that in the second state he will become... And if this is so, we say: At the coming of the second state, either the state is one of equality or one of preponderance, and the mentioned problem returns. Or it means that in the state of equality he is commanded in this state to bring about preponderance in the second state.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is Razi's fourth refutation of the Mu'tazila, focusing on the state of motives. He argues that whenever a person is obligated to perform an action, the motives (dawa'ī) for doing it or refraining from it are either equal (istiwā') or one is preponderant (rājiḥ). If they are equal, obligating the person to choose one over the other (tarjīḥ) is an impossible obligation because it requires combining equality and preponderance, which are contradictory. If one motive is preponderant, then the preponderant act is necessarily going to occur, meaning it cannot be caused by the agent's choice (since it is necessary in itself), so obligating it is beyond the agent's capacity. Conversely, obligating the outweighed act is impossible because it cannot occur. Razi then addresses a potential objection: perhaps the obligation applies not during the state of equality but in a later state. He refutes this by analyzing two possibilities: either the person is informed during equality that they will be obligated later, in which case there is no obligation at the moment; or they are commanded to bring about preponderance in the future while still in the state of equality. In both cases, the problem recurs: at the future moment, motives will again be either equal or preponderant, leading back to the same dilemma.

Key Terms
الداعي (motive)
الاستواء (equality)
الترجيح (preponderance)
الراجح (preponderant)
المرجوح (outweighed)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is denying human agency entirely, or that he claims God never obligates anything impossible.
Correction: Razi is not denying agency but logically refuting the specific Mu'tazilī condition that obligation requires the agent to have balanced motives. He shows that under that condition, obligation leads to contradiction or impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is obligating someone to act when motives are equal an impossible obligation?
A) Because the person is already doing the action.
B) Because it requires combining equality and preponderance, which are contradictory.
C) Because God does not command in such situations.
D) Because the person cannot understand the command.

Key Takeaway
Obligation is impossible in both states of motives—equal or preponderant—because each leads to logical contradiction or impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt paralyzed by two equally strong options and believed you were commanded to choose anyway? How does Razi's argument show that such a command would be impossible, and what does that reveal about your expectations of yourself?
?
Think of a time when you acted on a strong inclination and later felt fully responsible. Razi says the preponderant act is necessarily occurring. Does this change how you view your responsibility? Write down one specific decision you'll reconsider in light of this.
?
If obligation is impossible in both states, what does that imply about how you approach difficult moral choices? Will you still strive to obey God, and on what basis? Commit to one practical step to trust God's wisdom even when you cannot logically justify your obligation.

The Impossibility of Evil for God and the Ash'ari Response
Argument that evil acts are impossible for God and the Ash'ari answer that they imply no ignorance or need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that evil acts are impossible for God and how the Ash'ari school responds by denying the implication of ignorance or need.

Core Idea
Evil acts do not imply ignorance or need in God — the Ash'ari key rebuttal.

This passage from Imam Razi's Al-Arba'een presents an objection against the possibility of evil acts from God. The objector argues that such acts would imply ignorance or need in God, which are impossible, therefore the acts themselves are impossible and not within divine power. The Ash'ari response is then given: creating such things does not indicate ignorance or need in God. We will examine the logic and the defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal English translation. Let the argument's flow become clear.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'because' and 'so'.
2
Identify the objection's logical chain
Notice the premises: impossible acts are not in God's power; evil acts would imply ignorance or need (both impossible); therefore evil acts are impossible and not in God's power. Trace this chain.
3
Understand the Ash'ari response
The Ash'ari reject the premise that evil acts imply ignorance or need. They state simply that creating such things does not indicate those attributes. Reflect on why this refutes the objection.
The passage does not elaborate further; it is a direct denial of the implication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محال وكل محال فهو غير مقدور. وإنما قلنا إنه محال لأن صدور هذه الأشياء عن الله تعالى يستلزم الجهل أو الحاجة وكل واحد منهما محال. ومستلزم المحال محال. فصدور هذه الأشياء عن الله تعالى محال. وإنما قلنا: إن المحال غير مقدور، لأن المحال ما يمتنع وقوعه والمقدور هو الذي يمكن وقوعه. والجمع بينهما محال. والجواب على مذهبنا إن خلق شيء من الأشياء لا يدل في حق الله تعالى على الجهل ولا على الحاجة. فزال هذا السؤال.
Impossible, and everything impossible is not within [divine] power. We only say it is impossible because the emanation of these things from God Almighty necessitates ignorance or need, and each of them is impossible. The necessitator of the impossible is impossible. So the emanation of these things from God Almighty is impossible. We only say: The impossible is not within [divine] power, because the impossible is what cannot occur, and the [divinely] empowered [act] is what can occur. Combining them is impossible. The answer according to our school [Ash'ari] is that creating any of these things does not indicate, with respect to God Almighty, ignorance or need. So this objection is removed.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the Ash'ari response. The objector begins by stating that whatever is impossible (محال) is not within God's power (غير مقدور). Then he argues that evil acts are impossible because they would imply ignorance or need in God—both impossible. Therefore, such acts are impossible and hence not in God's power. The Ash'ari answer rejects the premise: creating such things does not imply ignorance or need in God, so the objection collapses. The argument follows a logical chain: impossibility of implication leads to impossibility of the act, but the Ash'ari deny the implication.

Key Terms
محال
مقدور
جاهل
حاجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that if God can do evil, He must be ignorant or needy.
Correction: The Ash'ari deny that implication; creating evil does not indicate ignorance or need in God.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response in this passage, why does creating evil not imply ignorance or need in God?
A) Because evil acts are never really evil.
B) Because the Ash'ari deny the implication entirely, though the passage does not explain why.
C) Because God is not subject to moral categories.
D) Because the objector's premise is false.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response in this passage is that creating evil does not indicate ignorance or need in God, thus removing the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought that a difficult event in your life meant that God was unaware of your situation or needed something from you? How does the Ash'ari response in this passage challenge that assumption?
?
If someone told you that the existence of evil proves that God is imperfect, could you use the argument from this passage to respond? Write down exactly what you would say, using the logic from the source.
?
Reflect on a time you felt that an action was too evil for God to allow. After this lesson, do you still believe that God's allowing that action would imply a defect in Him? What has changed in your understanding?

The Mu'tazilite View on Divine Power and Evil Acts
Mu'tazilite explanation that evil acts are within God's power in terms of capability but prevented by lack of motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite distinction between God's power being suitable for evil acts and the actual prevention of those acts due to lack of motive.

Core Idea
Evil acts are within God's power but lack His motive.

The Mu'tazilites offer a nuanced answer to the question of whether God can commit evil acts. They argue that while God's power is fully capable of creating such acts, He does not do so because there is no motive (dāʿī) to perform them. This passage presents that key distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it, then read the translation. Notice how the Mu'tazilites define 'being empowered' (muqaddara) in terms of suitability (ṣāliḥa).
The passage contrasts two ideas: the power's capability and the absence of motive.
2
Identify the key terms
Look at the terms 'muqaddara', 'ṣāliḥa', and 'dāʿī'. Understand how they relate: power is suitable, but motive is missing.
These terms are central to the Mu'tazilite position.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that evil acts are not impossible due to power, but due to lack of motive. Grasp this distinction clearly.
This avoids implying any deficiency in God's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما على مذهب المعتزلة فالجواب عن هذه الشبهة: إن معنى كون القبائح مقدرة صالحة لإيجادها. فالحاصل أن فعل هذه القبائح وإن كان ممتنعا لامتناع الداعي، لكنه غير ممتنع بالنظر إلى صلاحية القدرة.
And as for according to the school of the Mu'tazilites, the answer to this doubt is: The meaning of evil acts being [divinely] empowered is that [God's] power is suitable for bringing them into existence. So the conclusion is that the doing of these evil acts, even if it is prevented due to the absence of a motive, is not prevented with respect to the suitability of [His] power.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage gives the Mu'tazilite response to the same objection. They define 'being empowered' (مقدرة) as the power being suitable (صالحة) for bringing the act into existence. Thus, evil acts are within God's power in terms of capability, but they do not occur because there is no motive (داعي) to do them. So the impossibility is not from the power itself but from the lack of a motive. This distinguishes the Mu'tazilite view from the Ash'ari view on the nature of divine power.

Key Terms
مقدرة
صلاحية
داعي
قبائح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'impossible' in the context of God and evil acts means absolute impossibility.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites clarify that the impossibility is relative: it is impossible for God to do evil acts only because there is no motive, not because His power is insufficient.

Checkpoint
Why are evil acts not actually performed by God according to the Mu'tazilite answer?
A) Because God's power is not suitable for them.
B) Because there is no motive for God to do them.
C) Because they are logically impossible.
D) Because God is not the creator of evil.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites maintain that God's power is suitable for creating evil acts, but He does not perform them because He has no motive to do so.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Al-Ka'bi's Objection and the Ash'ari Response
Al-Ka'bi argues God cannot do acts like human acts; the Ash'ari reply that acts are movements and God can do all movements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ka'bi's objection that God cannot perform human acts because they are obedience or disobedience, and the Ash'ari response that reduces acts to physical motions, showing God's power over all movements.

Core Idea
Human acts are reducible to motions; God creates all motions.

Al-Ka'bi raises a powerful objection: if God is the creator of all acts, then He would be doing obedient and disobedient acts. But that is impossible for God. The Ash'ari response is subtle: it separates the physical reality of an act from its moral label. Let's see how this argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection, then answer.
2
Identify Al-Ka'bi's premise
Al-Ka'bi argues that human acts are either obedience or disobedience, both impossible for God. Understand why he thinks this proves God cannot create the same acts.
3
Understand the Ash'ari reduction
The Ash'ari response reduces 'obedience' and 'disobedience' to physical components: motion (حركة) and stillness (سكون). Note how this shift changes the argument.
The moral quality is not part of the act's essence.
4
Reflect on the implication
Since God is capable of all motions and stillnesses, He can create the exact same physical act as a human. The moral label depends on the agent's intention and command.
This is a key nuance in Ash'ari theology of human acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما (الكعبي) فإنه يقول: إنه تعالى غير قادر على مثل مقدور العبد، لأن فعل العبد إما طاعة أو معصية. وهما محالان على الله تعالى فثبت أنه تعالى غير قادر على مثل فعل العبد. والجواب: إن طاعة العبد ومعصيته إما حركة وإما سكون والله تعالى قادر على جميع الحركات والسكنات.
And as for al-Ka'bi, he says: He [God] Almighty is not capable of the like of what a servant is capable of, because the servant's act is either obedience or disobedience. And both are impossible with respect to God Almighty, so it is established that He Almighty is not capable of the like of the servant's act. The answer: The servant's obedience and disobedience are either motion or stillness, and God Almighty is capable of all motions and stillnesses.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ka'bi's objection and the Ash'ari response. Al-Ka'bi argues that God cannot do the same kind of acts as humans because human acts are categorized as obedience or disobedience, which are impossible for God. The Ash'ari answer reduces human acts to their physical components: motion (حركة) or stillness (سكون). Since God is capable of all motions and stillnesses, He can create the same physical act. The moral quality (obedience/disobedience) is not part of the act's essence but arises from the agent's intention and command, so it does not affect God's power over the physical action.

Key Terms
مقدور العبد
طاعة
معصية
حركة
سكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the moral categorization of an act (obedience/disobedience) defines its reality, making it impossible for God to create it.
Correction: The Ash'ari show that the act's reality is physical (motion/stillness), which God creates; the moral label depends on intention and command, not the act's essence.

Checkpoint
How does the Ash'ari answer reduce the act of the servant to something God can do?
A) By denying that God creates human acts at all.
B) By stating that obedience and disobedience are not real acts.
C) By reducing the act to motion or stillness, which God can create.
D) By claiming that God's acts are not comparable to human acts.

Key Takeaway
God's power encompasses all physical actions; moral labels depend on intention and command, not the act's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you see that sin as 'your' creation or as a movement God created and you misused? How would owning the movement as God's gift change your repentance?
?
If every motion—even your smallest gesture—is created by God, do you feel a greater sense of dependency or a greater burden of responsibility? Where does the Ash'ari distinction between creation and acquisition leave you?
?
Consider a habit you struggle with. Can you separate the physical motions (hand, tongue, eyes) from your intention? Would acknowledging that God creates the motions make you more or less determined to change your intention?
