Basran Objection and Ash'ari Response on Shared Power
Basran objection and Ash'ari response on shared agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Basran objection to shared divine-human agency and the Ash'ari response that a deterrent does not necessarily prevent an act when another agent wills its existence.

Core Idea
A conflict of motives does not force an impossibility if another agent's will causes the act.

This passage presents a crucial debate in Islamic theology on divine power and human agency. The Basrans (Mu'tazilites) argue that one act cannot be simultaneously under two agents because conflicting motives would produce a contradiction. The Ash'ari response challenges this by noting that the intention of one agent can override the deterrent of another.

Guided Path
1
Read the Basran argument
Read the first part of the passage where the Basrans state their position and their proof. Notice the assumption that a deterrent necessarily prevents the act.
2
Analyze the contradiction claim
The Basrans claim that if one agent has motive and the other deterrent, the act would both exist and not exist. Consider why they think this is impossible.
3
Study the Ash'ari response
The response rejects the necessity that a deterrent prevents the act. Understand the reasoning: the other agent's intention to bring about the act can serve as a cause for its existence.
This response introduces the concept of causal concurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما البصريون فقد سلموا أنه تعالى قادر على مثل مقدور العبد، إلا أنهم قالوا: إنه تعالى غير قادر على نفس مقدور العبد. واحتجوا عليه بأن ما كان مقدورا للقادر، فلا بد أن يحصل عندما يدعوه الداعي إلى فعله وأن لا يحصل عندما يصرفه الصارف عن فعله فلو فرضنا مقدورا واحدا بين قادرين وحصل الداعي إلى الفعل في حق أحدهما، وحصل الصارف عن الفعل في حق الثاني لزم أن يوجد ذلك الفعل وأن لا وهذا محال. فالقول بوجود مقدور بين قادرين محال. والجواب: أن من جوز وجود مقدور واحد لقادرين لم يسلم أنه يلزم من تحقيق الصارف في حق أحد القادرين عن الفعل أن لا يوجد الفعل، لاحتمال أن يكون قصد غيره إلى إيجاده سببا لوجوده.
And as for the Basrans, they conceded that He Almighty is capable of the like of what the servant is capable of, except that they said: He Almighty is not capable of the exact same act that the servant is capable of. They argued that what is capable for a capable agent must occur when the motive prompts him to do it and must not occur when a deterrent prevents him from doing it. So if we suppose a single capability between two capable agents, and the motive for the act occurs in respect of one of them, and the deterrent from the act occurs in respect of the other, then it would be necessary that that act both exist and not exist—and this is impossible. So the statement that there exists a capability between two capable agents is impossible. The answer: He who permits the existence of a single capability for two capable agents does not concede that the realization of a deterrent in respect of one of them necessitates that the act does not exist, because it is possible that the intention of the other to bring it about is a cause for its existence.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents the Basran (Mu'tazilite from Basra) objection and the Ash'ari response. The Basrans concede that God can create an act similar to a human's, but not the very same act. Their proof: if one act were under two agents, a conflict of motive and deterrent would force a contradiction. The Ash'ari answer rejects the necessity of the deterrent preventing the act, because the other agent's intention to bring it about overrides. The act may exist due to the other's will. This is an early discussion of causal concurrence.

Key Terms
مقدور واحد
قادرين
داعي
صارف
سبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A deterrent (ṣārif) always prevents an act from occurring, regardless of other agents.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that if another agent intends the act, that intention can be a cause for its existence, overriding the deterrent.

Checkpoint
What is the Basran argument against one act being under two agents?
A) The act would be impossible because it would require two origins.
B) If one agent has motive and another has deterrent, the act would both exist and not exist.
C) Two agents cannot have power over the same thing because power is exclusive.
D) The act would be multiple because each agent would produce a separate act.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response defends the possibility of two agents sharing one act by arguing that a deterrent does not always prevent the act if another agent wills it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a strong deterrent to do good—like fear or laziness—do you ever pause to consider that God might intend for that good to happen through you anyway? How can you align your will with His intention, trusting that He can override your obstacles?
?
This lesson argues that God's power and your power can coincide in the same act without contradiction. In your daily life, do you truly act as if every choice you make is simultaneously willed by God? Or do you compartmentalize your actions as purely your own? What would change if you consciously remembered that God's agency is directly involved in your every move?
?
The Basrans assumed a logical contradiction if two agents share one act, but the Ash'ari response shows that God's will can cause the act despite your deterrent. Is there a sin or good deed you have convinced yourself is impossible because of your circumstances? How might this teaching challenge your assumption that God cannot bring it about through you?

Mu'tazilite Proof for Tawallud and the Ash'ari Response
Mu'tazilite argument for tawallud from praise/blame and Ash'ari referral to creation of acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument from praise and blame for generated actions and how the Ash'ari respond by referring to the doctrine of creation of acts.

Core Idea
Moral accountability rests on God's creation of acts, not on generated effects.

This brief passage captures a key Mu'tazilite proof for tawallud (generated actions): if acts do not generate their consequences, praise and blame become meaningless. The Ash'ari reply directs us to an earlier discussion on the creation of acts, where the foundations of human accountability are laid. Let's examine this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the two-part structure: the Mu'tazilite claim and the Ash'ari reference.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite argument
Note that the Mu'tazilites tie tawallud to the moral value of praise, blame, reward, and punishment. Ask yourself why they think generation is necessary for these concepts.
3
Understand the Ash'ari response
The Ash'ari do not present a new argument here but refer back to the issue of creation of acts. This implies that their earlier refutation of the Mu'tazilite position on creating acts also applies here.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on how this exchange fits into the larger kalām discussion on human agency and divine causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدلال المعتزلة على القول بالتولد: إنما هو لحسن المدح والذم والثواب والعقاب. وقد تقدم الجواب عنه في مسألة خلق الأفعال.
And the Mu'tazilites' proof for the statement about generation is only for the sake of the goodness of praise and blame and reward and punishment. And the answer to it has already preceded in the issue of the creation of acts.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This brief passage states the Mu'tazilite argument for tawallud (generated actions): it is necessary to make moral praise, blame, reward, and punishment meaningful. If human actions do not generate effects, then humans are not truly responsible for the consequences of their acts. The Ash'ari responds by saying the answer was already given in the earlier discussion on the creation of acts (khalq al-af'al), where they argued that God creates all actions, including human acts, so moral accountability is based on acquisition (kasb) rather than generation.

D) In the issue of the impossibility of evil.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite argument from praise and blame for tawallud is refuted by the Ash'ari by reaffirming the doctrine of creation of acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

God's Will vs. Approval: Refuting Objections on Human Disbelief
Imam Razi refutes objections 4-6 and answers all six objections on divine will and disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi distinguishes between God's will (إرادة) and His approval (رضا) to refute objections that God's willing disbelief would lead to absurdities.

Core Idea
God wills all that occurs, but only loves what He commands.

This passage presents three powerful objections against the Ash'ari view that God wills everything, including disbelief, and Razi's crisp refutations. The nub of his response is a crucial distinction: divine will is not the same as divine pleasure. Understanding this distinction is key to resolving apparent tensions in divine predestination and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections (4-6)
Read the Arabic text and translation of the fourth, fifth, and sixth objections. Notice how each objection assumes that if God wills something, He must also approve of it.
The fourth objection links decree to approval; the fifth argues that creating disbelief makes faith impossible; the sixth cites Quranic verses.
2
Study Razi's answers
Read the answers to all six objections. Focus on the distinction between will (إرادة) and approval (رضا), and between decree (قضاء) and its effects.
Razi answers the fourth by saying we are pleased with the attribute, not its effect; the sixth by defining approval as refraining from objection.
3
Reflect on the key distinction
Understanding that God's will encompasses all things, but His approval is limited to what He commands, resolves the apparent contradiction. Reflect on how this preserves divine omnipotence and justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الرابعة والعشرون الكفر من الكافرين لكان الكافر مطيعا لله تعالى بكفره وذلك باطل بالاتفاق. الشبهة الرابعة : الرضا بقضاء الله تعالى واجب بالإجماع، فلو كان الكفر بقضاء الله تعالى لوجب الرضا بالكفر، لكن هذا باطل لأن الرضا بالكفر كفر. الشبهة الخامسة : لو أراد الله تعالى الكفر من الكافر، وخلق الكفر في الكافر، لكان تكليف الكافر بالإيمان تكليف بما لا يطاق. وهذا باطل بالعقل والسمع. أما العقل : فلأن كل إنسان يجد بالضرورة من نفسه أنه إن أراد الطاعة، يمكنه فعلها فكيف يقال : إنه غير متمكن من الطاعة، أنه يجد بالضرورة ثبوت هذه مع المكنة؟ أما السمع : فقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَمَاذَا عَلَيْهِمْ لَو ءَامَنُوا بِالله ا [النساء : ٣٩] وفا هم عَنِ التذكرة مُعرِضِينَ ﴾ [المدثر : ٤٩] وكذا القول في جملة الآيات الدالة على أن الإنسان غير عاجز عن الإتيان بالطاعات. الشبهة السادسة : التمسك بقوله تعالى : ﴿وَلَا يَرْضَى لِعِبَادِهِ الكفر [الزمر : ٧] وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلّمًا لِلْعِبَادِ الله [غافر: ٣١] وبقوله يَهْدِي مَن يَشَاءُ [البقرة : ١٤٢] . والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : لا نسلم أن الأمر بالشيء يستدعي إرادة المأمور فإن النزاع ما وقع إلا فيه. وعن الشبهة الثانية إنا سنقيم الدلالة القاطعة - إن شاء الله تعالى - على أن القبيح لا يقبح إلا بالشرع، وعلى هذا الأصل فإنه لا يقبح من الله تعالى شيء. وعن الشبهة الثالثة : إن الطاعة موافقة للأمر لا موافقة للإرادة. وعن الشبهة الرابعة : القضاء صفة الله ونحن راضون بقضاء الله تعالى. أي راضون بصفة الله تعالى، وأما الكفر فليس هو نفس قضاء الله تعالى، بل هو مقتضي قضاء الله تعالى ولم يدل الدليل على أنه واجب الرضى بكل شيء قضى الله تعالى به. وعن الشبهة الخامسة : المعارضة بالوجوه الستة التي ذكرناها في خلق الأفعال. وعن الشبهة السادسة : إن الرضا والمحبة ترك الاعتراض، والكفر والمعاصي وإن كانا بإرادة الله تعالى لكنهما ليستا بمحبة الله تعالى ولا برضاه.
The twenty-fourth issue: Disbelief from the disbelievers — then the disbeliever would be obedient to God Almighty through his disbelief, and that is invalid by consensus. The fourth objection: Being pleased with the decree of God Almighty is obligatory by consensus. If disbelief were by the decree of God Almighty, then being pleased with disbelief would be obligatory, but this is invalid because being pleased with disbelief is disbelief. The fifth objection: If God Almighty willed disbelief from the disbeliever and created disbelief in the disbeliever, then obligating the disbeliever with faith would be an obligation beyond capacity. This is invalid by reason and revelation. As for reason: because every person necessarily finds from himself that if he wills obedience, he can do it. So how can it be said that he is not capable of obedience? He necessarily finds the existence of ability with capability. As for revelation: His statement, exalted: "And what harm would it do them if they believed in Allah" [Quran 4:39] and "But they turn away from the reminder" [Quran 74:49]. And similarly for the verses indicating that man is not incapable of performing acts of obedience. The sixth objection: Clinging to His statement, exalted: "And He does not approve for His servants disbelief" [Quran 39:7] and His statement, exalted: "And Allah does not intend injustice for the servants" [Quran 40:31] and His statement: "He guides whom He wills" [Quran 2:142]. The answer to the first objection: We do not concede that commanding a thing necessitates willing the commanded thing, for the dispute is only about that. And regarding the second objection: We will establish conclusive proof — God willing — that evil is only evil by divine law, and on this principle nothing is evil from God Almighty. And regarding the third objection: Obedience is compliance with the command, not compliance with the will. And regarding the fourth objection: The decree is an attribute of God, and we are pleased with God's decree — that is, we are pleased with God's attribute. As for disbelief, it is not itself the decree of God Almighty, but rather that which is necessitated by the decree of God Almighty, and no proof has indicated that it is obligatory to be pleased with everything that God has decreed. And regarding the fifth objection: Counter-argument with the six faces that we mentioned in the creation of actions. And regarding the sixth objection: Approval and love [mean] refraining from objection. Disbelief and acts of disobedience, although they occur by God's will, are not objects of God's love nor of His approval.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes several objections against the Ash'ari position that God wills all things, including disbelief, while not approving of it. The passage begins with a concluding sentence from a previous objection (likely the third) that if disbelief were from the disbeliever then he would be obedient — which is rejected by consensus. Then the fourth objection argues that being pleased with God's decree is obligatory; if disbelief is decreed, then one must be pleased with it, which would be disbelief itself. The fifth objection argues that if God wills and creates disbelief, then commanding faith is an impossible burden (taklif ma la yutaq), which is false by reason and revelation — reason shows humans feel capable of obedience, and revelation provides verses indicating ability. The sixth objection cites Quranic verses that seem to show God does not approve of disbelief, does not intend injustice, and guides whom He wills. The answers follow: For the first objection, Razi denies that commanding requires willing the commanded act, as the dispute is precisely about that. For the second, he promises a proof that evil is only evil by divine law, so God cannot be deemed evil. For the third, he distinguishes obedience as compliance with command, not with will. For the fourth, he explains that the decree is God's attribute, and we are pleased with the attribute, but disbelief is the effect of the decree, and there is no obligation to be pleased with every effect. For the fifth, he refers to earlier arguments on the creation of actions. For the sixth, he clarifies that divine approval means refraining from objection, and disbelief, though willed by God, is not loved or approved by Him. The overall argument preserves God's universal will while denying His approval of evil.

Key Terms
قضاء
إرادة
رضا
طاعة
كفر
تكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's willing something implies that He approves of it and loves it.
Correction: Razi shows that divine will (إرادة) is distinct from divine approval (رضا) and love (محبة). God wills all events, including disbelief, but only approves and loves what He commands.

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the objection that being pleased with God's decree (قضاء) would require being pleased with disbelief?
A) He says disbelief itself is the decree, so we must be pleased with it.
B) He distinguishes between the decree as God's attribute and disbelief as its effect, and says we are only pleased with the attribute.
C) He denies that disbelief is decreed.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi defends divine omnipotence and justice by distinguishing God's universal will from His limited approval, and decree from its effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a personal tragedy or a situation you deeply dislike, do you find yourself resenting God's decree? How does this lesson's distinction between decree as an attribute and its effects challenge you to realign your heart with trust in God while still acknowledging your human emotions? What will you do the next time such resentment arises?
?
This lesson teaches that obedience is compliance with God's command, not with His will. Where in your life are you using God's will as an excuse for disobedience? For example, do you ever think 'it's meant to be' to avoid following a clear command? What command have you been neglecting because you assume God's will covers it?
?
Since God wills disbelief but does not approve of it, how do you view your own sins? Do you take them lightly because 'God willed it'? Or do you feel the weight of disobeying what God loves, even though He allowed it? How can you cultivate hatred for sin in your heart without falling into despair over God's decree?

The Fifth Proof: God Creates All Acts, So Not for Benefit
Razi presents the fifth proof against divine motivation by purposes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical implication of God being the sole creator of all acts: that His creative activity cannot be constrained by considerations of benefit or purpose.

Core Idea
If God creates both good and evil, He cannot be acting for creatures' benefit.

This lesson presents the fifth of Razi's proofs for the Ash'ari position on divine attributes. Building on the established doctrine that only God brings actions into existence, Razi draws a powerful conclusion: if God creates everything – even evil and unbelief – then His act of creation cannot be motivated by a desire to benefit creatures. This argument refutes the Mu'tazilite notion that God must do what is best for His creatures.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi begins by referencing a previously established point about the creation of actions.
The proof is concise; each sentence carries weight.
2
Identify the premise
The proof rests on the claim that only God brings things into existence. Confirm that you understand this premise and its basis in Ash'ari theology.
3
Trace the implication
Since good and evil both come from God, consider what this means for the idea that God creates only for benefit. If He were motivated by benefit, would He create evil?
This is the key logical move.
4
State the conclusion
Razi concludes that God's being Creator cannot be conditional on considering benefits. Write the conclusion in your own words.
The conclusion is a direct consequence of the premise and observation.
5
Reflect on the significance
This argument strengthens the Ash'ari view that God's actions are not subject to external purposes. Consider how it differs from the Mu'tazilite position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : قد بينا في مسألة خلق الأفعال أنه لا موجد إلا الله تعالى، وإذا كان كذلك كان الخير والشر والكفر والإيمان حاصلا بإيجاده وتخليقه وتكوينه . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك امتنع توقف كونه تعالى خالقا وموجدا على رعاية المصالح والأغراض.
The fifth proof: We have explained in the question of the creation of actions that there is no bringer-into-existence except God Most High. And if that is so, then good and evil, unbelief and faith, occur through His bringing-into-existence, His creating, and His forming. And if the matter is so, it is impossible that His being Creator and Bringer-into-existence be dependent on consideration of benefits and purposes.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof, which draws on the earlier discussion (in another question) about the creation of actions (khalaq al-af'al). The author asserts that only God brings things into existence; all actions, including evil and unbelief, are created by God. Since God creates both beneficial and harmful things, it cannot be said that His creation is motivated by consideration of creatures' benefits, because if it were, He would not create evil and unbelief. Therefore, God's creative act cannot be dependent on purposes or benefits.

Key Terms

موجد
الخير والشر
الكفر والإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof means God approves of evil.
Correction: The proof only shows that God's act of creation is not motivated by creatures' benefit; it does not imply moral approval of evil. Moral evaluation is separate from divine creative causation.

Checkpoint
Why does the fifth proof claim that God's creation cannot be for the benefit of creatures?
A) Because creatures do not actually benefit from anything.
B) Because God creates both good and evil, so He cannot be motivated only by benefit.
C) Because the Mu'tazilites believe creatures create their own acts.
D) Because all benefits are created by God.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof shows that God's sole creation of both good and evil logically entails that His creative act is not dependent on consideration of creatures' benefits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
This proof shows that God is not constrained by external purposes. How does that free you from trying to judge God's actions by your own sense of fairness? What assumption about God must you abandon to truly submit to His will?

The Second Sophism: The Possibility of Universal Lying
Razi presents a sophism that the intention to lie can occur in all reporters, so tawatur is not certain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that the possibility of lying in each reporter implies the possibility of all reporters lying together, challenging the certainty of tawatur.

Core Idea
If lying is possible for each, it is possible for all — so tawatur's certainty is questioned.

In this lesson, we examine the second sophism presented by Imam al-Razi against the validity of tawatur. He argues that since the intention to lie can arise in any individual, and its occurrence in one does not prevent its occurrence in another, it is logically possible for all reporters to lie simultaneously. This challenges the claim that tawatur produces certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for Razi's dense style. Then read the literal translation.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then'.
2
Identify the key premise
Find the sentence that states the cause of lying: 'an intention to lie occurs in his heart'. Understand that this intention can come from God, the servant, or without any cause.
Razi covers all possibilities to make the argument universal.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the argument: (a) The intention is possible for each reporter. (b) Its occurrence in one does not prevent its occurrence in another. (c) Therefore it is possible for all to have that intention. (d) If all share the intention, they all lie. (e) So it is possible for all to lie together.
The conclusion is that it is not impossible for all to lie.
4
Grasp the implication for tawatur
If it is possible that all reporters lied, then the information they convey is not certain. Razi argues that claiming certainty despite this possibility is a mistake.
This directly attacks the foundation of tawatur as a source of certain knowledge.
5
Reflect on responses
Consider how a defender of tawatur might respond. Typically, they would argue that while it is possible in theory, in practice it does not happen due to divine protection or other factors.
Razi's point is that the mere possibility undermines logical certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : هي أن الإنسان إنما يقدم على الكذب لأنه حصل في قلبه إرادة أن يكذب، وحصول هذه الإرادات في القلب إما أن يكون من الله تعالى أو من العبد أو حدثت لا عن محدث ومؤثر، وعلى التقديرات الثلاثة فكل ذلك جائز في حق كل واحد من هؤلاء المخبرين ولم يكن حصول هذا السبب في حق بعضهم مانعا ومنافيًا من حصوله في حق الباقين، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب القطع بأنه لا يمتنع حصول ذلك السبب الداعي إلى الكذب في حق الكل، وبتقدير وقوع الاشتراك في تلك الإرادة وجب وقوع الاشتراك في الكذب والمعلق على سبب جائز الوجود كان هو أيضًا جائز الوقوع فثبت أن اشتراك الكل في الكذب غير ممتنع البتة، وإذا ثبت هذا كان القطع بأن ذلك الجائز لم يقع خطأ وبعيدًا.
The second sophism: It is that a person only proceeds to lying because an intention to lie occurs in his heart. The occurrence of these intentions in the heart is either from God Almighty, or from the servant, or it occurs without a determiner and effecter. On all three suppositions, all of that is possible in respect of each one of these reporters, and the occurrence of this cause in respect of some of them is not a preventer or contradictory to its occurrence in respect of the others. If the matter is so, then it must be concluded that the occurrence of that cause which leads to lying is not impossible in respect of all. And if we suppose the sharing in that intention, then the sharing in lying must occur. And that which is conditional upon a cause whose existence is possible is also possible of occurrence. So it is established that the sharing of all in lying is not impossible at all. And when this is established, then the certainty that that possible thing did not occur is a mistake and far-fetched.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This is the second sophism of the first maqam. It focuses on the cause of lying: the intention (irada) to lie. This intention, according to the mutakallimun, can be from God, from the servant, or without any cause. In any case, it is possible for each individual. Since the occurrence in one does not prevent its occurrence in another, it is possible for all to have that intention. If they all share that intention, they would all lie. Therefore, it is not impossible for all to lie together. Thus, claiming certainty that they did not lie is a mistake.

Key Terms
إرادة الكذب
المحدث
الجواز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sophism only applies if the intention to lie comes from the servant.
Correction: Razi explicitly covers all three possibilities (from God, from servant, or without any cause) and shows the argument holds in each case.

Checkpoint
According to this sophism, what is the cause of lying?
A) External compulsion.
B) An intention to lie in the heart.
C) Ignorance.
D) Social pressure.

Key Takeaway
The sophism shows that if lying is individually possible for each reporter, then it is not impossible for all to lie together, so tawatur cannot provide absolute certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a widely held belief you maintain simply because 'everyone says it'—perhaps a common religious practice or a theological position. If you learned that it is logically possible for all those transmitters to be mistaken or lying, would your certainty waver? What specific step in Razi's argument forces you to reconsider the foundation of that belief?
?
The sophism suggests that the intention to lie can arise from God or from the servant himself. If you were honest, which of these two possibilities seems more plausible in your own acts of deception? Does this passage make you uncomfortable about the role of your own will in sin? How will you respond to that discomfort tomorrow?

First Doubt: Taklif Invalid Due to Jabr
The first objection against prophethood: taklif is invalid because of determinism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the determinist objection that divine obligation is inherently unjust, and why its proponents believe this undermines prophethood.

Core Idea
If humans have no free will, commanding them is absurd — like ordering a tied man to fly.

This lesson introduces the first major objection against the necessity of prophethood: the argument from determinism (jabr). The challengers argue that since all acts are created by God, humans lack genuine ability, making any divine command unjust and irrational. They illustrate this with vivid analogies to show the repugnance of commanding the powerless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Let the author's reasoning come through.
Notice the two-part structure: first establishing jabr, then showing its consequence for taklif.
2
Identify the two main claims
The passage claims: (1) jabr is true, and (2) if jabr is true then taklif is invalid. Pause to see how each claim is supported.
3
Examine the analogies
Pay attention to the analogies: the bound person thrown from a cliff, the blind man, the paralytic. What do they illustrate about the perceived injustice of taklif?
The analogies appeal to ‘badāhat al-ʿaql’ – self-evident reason.
4
Reflect on the underlying premise
Ask yourself: what must be true for an obligation to be fair? The opponent assumes that genuine ability to choose is necessary.
5
Connect to prophethood
The objection concludes that since taklif is invalid, prophethood (which conveys obligation) is also invalid. Understand this link.
This is the first of six doubts against prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما منكرو النبوة فقد احتجوا بوجوه : الشبهة الأولى : قالوا : التكليف باطل، فالبعثة أيضا باطلة. أما المقدمة الأولى : - وهي أن التكليف باطل - فقد احتجوا عليه بوجوه : الأول : أن الجبر حق، فالتكليف باطل، وإنما قلنا : إن الجبر حق وذلك لأنا بينا في مسألة خلق الأفعال ان أفعال العباد مخلوقة لله تعالى وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فحال ما خلق الله تعالى تلك الأفعال لا يتمكن العبد من ترك الفعل، وحال ما لا يخلقها فيهم لا يتمكن من الفعل فثبت أن على هذا التقدير لا يكون العبد قادرًا البتة لا على الفعل ولا على الترك. وإنما قلنا : إنه لما كان الجبر حقا كان التكليف باطلا لأنا نعلم ببداهة العقل أن من قيد يدي إنسان ورجليه بالقيود الشديدة ثم ألقاه من شاهق جبل، ثم يقول له : قف في الهواء وإلا عذبتك عذابًا شديدًا مؤبداً ، فإن هذا مستقبح في بداهة العقل، ولو أنه كلف الأعمى بنقط المصاحف وكلف المفلوج أن يطير في الهواء، فجميع العقلاء يدركون قبح ذلك في بداهة عقولهم، ويقطعون بأن هذا لا يليق بأحكم الحاكمين، فثبت بما ذكرنا أن الجبر حق، وثبت أنه متى كان الجبر حقا كان التكليف باطلًا.
And as for those who deny prophethood, they have argued with doubts: The first doubt: they said: Taklif (obligation) is invalid, so prophethood (al-biʿtha) is also invalid. As for the first premise—which is that taklif is invalid—they have argued for it with proofs: The first: that jabr (determinism) is true, so taklif is invalid. And we say: jabr is true because we have shown in the issue of the creation of acts that the acts of servants are created by God most high. If that is so, then when God creates those acts, the servant cannot refrain from the act, and when He does not create them in them, they cannot perform the act. So it is established that on this basis the servant has no ability at all, neither for the act nor for refraining. And we say: since jabr is true, taklif is invalid because we know by the first principles of reason that if someone ties a person's hands and feet with tight bonds, then throws him from a high mountain, then says to him: 'Stay in the air or I will punish you with an everlasting severe punishment,' that is repugnant in the first principles of reason. And if he were to obligate a blind man to dot the copies of the Quran, and obligate a paralyzed person to fly in the air, all rational people perceive the repugnance of that in their first principles and are certain that this is not befitting the Wisest of judges. So it is established by what we have mentioned that jabr is true, and it is established that whenever jabr is true, taklif is invalid.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage presents the first doubt (shubha) of those who deny prophethood. They argue that taklif (divine obligation) is invalid, therefore prophethood (which exists to convey taklif) is also invalid. To prove the invalidity of taklif, they give six proofs. This lesson covers the first proof: that jabr (determinism) is true, making taklif unjust. The proof proceeds in two stages. First, they argue that human acts are created by God, so humans have no genuine ability—neither to do nor to refrain—contradicting the condition for valid obligation. Second, they illustrate the absurdity of commanding the powerless with analogies: tying a person and throwing him off a cliff then commanding him to stay in the air, or commanding a blind man to dot Quran copies and a paralytic to fly. All rational people see such commands as repugnant, so taklif under determinism is similarly repugnant and not befitting God's wisdom. The argument concludes that since jabr is true, taklif must be invalid.

Key Terms
تكليف (taklif)
جبر (jabr)
بداهة العقل (badāhat al-ʿaql)
خلق الأفعال (khalq al-afʿāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first proof given by the deniers of prophethood against taklif?
A) Taklif has no benefit
B) God's foreknowledge makes taklif pointless
C) Jabr (determinism) is true, so taklif is unjust
D) Taklif distracts from God's love

Key Takeaway
The determinist objection holds that if God determines all actions, human obligation is unjust and irrational, thus prophethood is unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command from Allah that feels impossible, do you silently accuse Him of injustice, like those who argue that jabr makes taklif invalid? What specific command have you made excuses for recently, believing you 'can't' obey? Commit today to stop hiding behind 'I can't' and instead ask Allah for the ability He has promised to provide.
?
The deniers used the analogy of a bound man thrown from a cliff ordered to stay in the air. When you feel trapped by your circumstances—your past, your habits, your environment—do you use that as a justification to disobey? Honestly examine your heart: are you truly incapable, or are you refusing to take responsibility? Ask Allah to remove the blindfold of self-deception.
?
If you truly believed that every action you do—including the sinful ones—is created by Allah and you have no power over it, would you still feel remorse and seek forgiveness? The doubt in this lesson exposes the danger of using divine decree as an excuse for sin. Will you stop using 'it was written' as a shield for your negligence and instead repent sincerely, knowing that you are accountable?

The Third Proof: Taklif Impossible Due to Motives
Argues that taklif is impossible because motives are either equal or one outweighs, making action impossible or necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the state of motives (dawāʿī) renders taklif impossible, both when motives are equal and when one outweighs the other.

Core Idea
If motives are equal, action can't happen; if one outweighs, action is forced. Taklif is thus impossible.

Imam al-Razi presents a logical argument against the possibility of religious obligation (taklif) from the nature of human motives. He argues that in any situation, the motives for an action and its opposite are either equal or one is stronger. Both cases lead to a contradiction if taklif is posited. This argument relies on a deterministic view of motives as the direct cause of action, a key theme in the Ash'ari discourse on qadar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Note the logical structure: two scenarios are distinguished.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage divides the state of motives into two: equality (istiwāʾ) and preponderance (rujḥān). Understand what each means.
Equality means the motive to act and the motive to refrain are exactly balanced.
3
Analyze the equality scenario
In equality, action requires preponderance, but equality means no preponderance. Thus action is impossible, making a command to act contradictory.
4
Analyze the preponderance scenario
When one motive outweighs, that act becomes necessary and its opposite impossible. Commanding the necessary is redundant, commanding the impossible is absurd.
5
Draw the conclusion
Both scenarios lead to the impossibility of taklif. Reflect on why this matters for the debate on divine justice and human responsibility.
This argument presupposes motives are the sole cause of action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن التكليف إما أن يتوجه حال استواء الداعي إلى الفعل والترك، أو حال رجحان أحد الداعيين على الآخر، أما حال الاستواء فمحال؛ لأن الفعل ترجح، وحصول الرجحان حال حصول الاستواء جمع بين النقيضين، وأما حال الرجحان فمحال ؛ لأن الراجح واجب والمرجوح ممتنع - على ما بينا تقرير هذه المقدمة في مواضع - وإن وقع التكليف بالراجح كان ذلك تكليفا بإيقاع شيء واجب الوقوع، وإن كان تكليفا بالمرجوح كان ذلك تكليفا بإيقاع ممتنع الوقوع، وتمام هذا الوجه قد تقدم في مسألة خلق الأفعال.
The third: that taklif is either directed when the motive toward the act and refraining are equal, or when one of the two motives outweighs the other. As for the state of equality, it is impossible, because the act [requires] preponderance, and the occurrence of preponderance in the state of equality is a combination of two contradictories. As for the state of preponderance, it is impossible, because the preponderant is necessary and the outweighed is impossible—according to what we have explained of the establishment of this premise in other places. And if taklif occurs for the preponderant, that is a command to do something necessary of occurrence. And if it is a command for the outweighed, that is a command to do something impossible of occurrence. And the completeness of this point has already been presented in the issue of the creation of acts.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof against taklif, based on the state of motives (dawāʿī). It divides the scenario into two possibilities: (1) the motives for action and inaction are equal, or (2) one motive outweighs the other. In the first case, equality of motives makes action impossible because action requires a preponderant motive; if both are equal, preponderance cannot occur, so action is impossible. In the second case, if one motive outweighs, the preponderant act becomes necessary (wājib) and its opposite impossible (mumtaniʿ). Thus commanding the preponderant is redundant (commanding what must happen) and commanding the outweighed is impossible (commanding what cannot happen). In both cases taklif is invalid. The argument references their earlier work on the creation of acts for a fuller discussion.

Key Terms
داعي (dāʿī)
استواء (istiwāʾ)
رجحان (rujḥān)
راجح (rājiḥ)
مرجوح (marjūḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That taklif is always possible because God can command anything, regardless of the agent's motives.
Correction: The argument assumes motives are the efficient cause of action; if motives are equal, no action can occur; if one outweighs, action is determined. Therefore, taklif would command either the impossible or the necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why is taklif impossible when motives are equal?
A) The act is forbidden
B) The act would be unjust
C) Action requires a preponderant motive, so equality prevents any action
D) The act is already known by God

Key Takeaway
The third proof uses the state of motives to argue that taklif is logically impossible, as it would require commanding either the necessary or the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever tell yourself 'I couldn't help it'? This proof says that your stronger motive made the sin necessary. If that is true, then how can you be blameworthy? But you know in your heart that you could have chosen otherwise. What does that contradiction reveal about this argument and about your own accountability?
?
Imagine standing before Allah with this precise proof: 'My motive for obedience was weaker than my desire, so my disobedience was necessary.' Would that excuse satisfy your own conscience? Or do you feel the weight of a choice you could have made, a choice that this reasoning denies? How do you respond to that tension?
?
Think of a moment when you had two equally strong options—for example, to speak the truth or to stay silent. Did you freeze, unable to act, as this proof predicts? Or did you still choose one? Your own experience may contradict the premise. If so, what does that tell you about the validity of this proof?

Second and Third Arguments Against Mu'tazila on Forgiveness
Imam Razi presents two logical arguments against the Mu'tazila interpretation of Quran 4:48.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two logical arguments (reductio and obligation-based) that Imam Razi uses to critique the Mu'tazila interpretation of Quran 4:48, and why he calls them dialectical (إلزامات).

Core Idea
Imam Razi uses the Mu'tazila's own premises to show their interpretation is self-defeating.

In this passage, Imam Razi continues his critique of the Mu'tazila interpretation of 'And He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills.' He presents two powerful logical arguments that challenge the Mu'tazila claim that the verse refers only to repentant sinners and minor sins. The first argument is a reductio showing their interpretation makes the distinction between shirk and other sins meaningless; the second argument points out that forgiveness after repentance is obligatory in Mu'tazila theology, but the verse suspends it on God's will, which contradicts obligation. However, Imam Razi notes these are dialectical arguments based on Mu'tazila premises he does not accept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its English translation carefully to grasp the two arguments presented by Imam Razi.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'لو' and 'فلا'.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find the second argument (ثانيها) and third argument (ثالثها). Note how each argument is structured: the second uses a reductio ad absurdum, the third uses a contradiction between obligation and divine will.
The second argument is about the uselessness of the division, the third about suspension on will.
3
Understand the dialectical nature
Read Imam Razi's concluding remark: these are إلزامات (dialectical conclusions) built on Mu'tazila premises. He does not affirm these premises himself, but uses them to refute the Mu'tazila from within their own framework.
This is a common method in Islamic philosophical theology (kalam) known as ilzam.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on how these arguments fit into the larger discussion of divine forgiveness, human responsibility, and the nature of sin. Consider why the Mu'tazila might have held their position and how Razi's critique exposes internal tensions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة والثلاثون
وثانيها : أنه لو كان قوله تعالى: ﴿وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ﴾ معناه أنه يغفر لمن يستحق المغفرة كالتائبين وأصحاب الصغائر، لم يبق لتمييز الشرك عن غيره فائدة؛ لأنه تعالى يغفر ما دون الشرك لمن يستحق العقاب ولا يغفر لمن يستحق العقاب، فكذلك يغفر الشرك لمن لا يستحق العقاب ولا يغفره لمن يستحق العقاب فلا يبقى البتة لهذا التقسيم فائدة.
وثالثها: أن غفران صاحب الكبيرة وصاحب الصغيرة بعد التوبة واجب والواجب لا يجوز تعليقه على المشيئة؛ لأن المعلق على المشيئة هو الذي إن شاء فعله و إن شاء تركه والواجب هو الذي لا بد من فعله شاء أم أبي، والمغفرة المذكورة في الآية معلقة على المشيئة فلا يجوز أن يكون المراد منها هو المغفرة للتائب وصاحب الصغيرة.
واعلم أن هذه الوجوه بأسرها إلزامات مبنية على قول المعتزلة : إنه يجب الغفران للتائب، وصاحب الصغيرة ونحن لا نقول بذلك.
The Thirty-Fifth Issue. Second: If the meaning of His statement, the Exalted, 'And He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' is that He forgives those who deserve forgiveness, such as the repentant and the committers of minor sins, then there would be no benefit in distinguishing shirk from other [sins]; because He, the Exalted, forgives what is less than shirk for those who deserve punishment, and He does not forgive for those who deserve punishment. Likewise, He forgives shirk for those who do not deserve punishment and does not forgive it for those who deserve punishment. Thus, there remains absolutely no benefit in this division. Third: That the forgiveness of the major sinner and the minor sinner after repentance is obligatory, and the obligatory cannot be suspended upon will; because that which is suspended upon will is that which if He wills He does, and if He wills He leaves, while the obligatory is that which must be done whether He wills or dislikes. The forgiveness mentioned in the verse is suspended upon will, so it is not permissible that what is intended by it is forgiveness for the repentant and the minor sinner. And know that all these arguments are dialectical conclusions built upon the statement of the Mu'tazila that forgiveness for the repentant and the minor sinner is obligatory, and we do not say that.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments against the Mu'tazila interpretation of Quran 4:48. The first argument (ثانيها) is a logical reductio: if the verse meant forgiveness only for those who deserve it (like repentant and minor sinners), then there would be no benefit in distinguishing shirk from other sins, because the same logic would apply—God forgives shirk for those who don't deserve punishment and doesn't forgive it for those who do, making the division pointless. The second argument (ثالثها) is based on the concept of obligation: forgiveness after repentance is obligatory (واجب) according to the Mu'tazila, but the verse suspends forgiveness on God's will (مشيئة), which is incompatible with obligation. Finally, Imam Razi notes that both arguments are dialectical (إلزامات), assuming the Mu'tazila premise that forgiveness for the repentant is obligatory—a premise he does not accept.

صغيرة

Correction: Imam Razi explicitly states that these are dialectical conclusions (إلزامات) built on Mu'tazila premises, and he does not accept that forgiveness after repentance is obligatory.

Checkpoint

B) Because God would forgive everyone equally regardless of sin
C) Because the concept of deserving punishment is irrelevant
D) Because the verse mentions shirk specifically

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi uses the Mu'tazila's own premises to show that their interpretation of Quran 4:48 leads to logical absurdities or contradictions, but he does not affirm those premises himself.

The First Proof Against Muwāzanah: The Prior and Subsequent
Razi presents the first of three logical proofs against muwāzanah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first logical proof that refutes the doctrine of balancing (muwāzanah) by analyzing the relationship between prior good deeds and subsequent sins.

Core Idea
The prior good deed either prevents the sin or does not; either way, balancing fails.

Imam Razi begins his refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of balancing by examining the logical possibilities between a prior good deed and a subsequent sin. This first proof shows that regardless of whether the prior prevents the subsequent or not, the idea of mutual cancellation leads to a contradiction. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two main branches of the argument: either the prior prevents the subsequent or it does not.
The argument uses a case-by-case analysis (taqsim).
2
Understand the first branch
If the prior prevents the subsequent, then the subsequent never exists, so no balancing (no sin to cancel the good).
This branch leads directly to the falsity of muwāzanah.
3
Understand the second branch
If the prior does not prevent the subsequent, then the subsequent comes to exist. Then either it cancels the prior or it does not. Both sub-branches lead to no balancing.
If it cancels, the prior becomes overcome, so it cannot cancel the sin. If it does not cancel, both coexist without conflict—so no mutual cancellation.
4
Conclude the proof
Razi concludes that this strong proof establishes the falsity of muwāzanah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السادسة والثلاثون
فنقول : القول بالموازنة باطل، ويدل عليه وجوه :
الأول: أن ثبوت السابق إما أن يمنع الطارئ من الدخول في الوجود، أو لا يمنعه، فإن منعه لم يدخل الطارئ في الوجود، و إذا لم يوجد الطارئ، بقي السابق كما كان، وهذا يمنع من الإحباط. وأما إن قلنا : إن السابق لم يمنع الطارئ من الدخول في الوجود، بل دخل في الوجود، فهذا الطارئ إما أن يؤثر في عدم السابق أو لا يؤثر فيه، فإن أثر فيه صار الطارئ غالبًا والسابق مغلوبًا، وعند صيرورة السابق مغلوبا امتنع أن يؤثر في إعدام الطارئ، فلم تكن الموازنة حاصلة، و إن قلنا بأن الطارئ بعد دخوله في الوجود لم يؤثر في إعدام السابق، فعلى هذا فقد وجد الطارئ مع السابق واجتمعا في الوجود، وعلى هذا التقدير لم يحصل بينهما منافاة أصلا، وإذا كان كذلك امتنع أن يكون أحدهما مؤثرًا في عدم الآخر، وفي زواله، فثبت بهذا البرهان القوي فساد القول بالموازنة.
الوجه الثاني في بطلانه هو أنه لو كان كل واحد من السابق والطارئ، مؤثرًا في عدم الآخر لكان تأثير كل واحد منهما في الآخر إما أن يكون معا، أو على التعاقب، والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بكون كل واحد منهما مؤثرا في عدم الآخر. وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع حصول ذلك معاً ؛ لأن المؤثر في عدم كل واحد منهما وجود الآخر، فلو حصل العدمان معا لحصل الوجودان معا حال حصول العدمين ؛ لأن العلة واجبة الحصول عند حصول المعلول فيلزم كونهما موجودين حال كونهما معدومين، وهو محال .
وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع حصول ذلك على التعاقب لا معا؛ لأنه يقتضي أن يعود المغلوب بعد صيرورته مغلوبًا غالبًا، وذلك محال.
الوجه الثالث : وهو أن شرط طريان أحد الضدين زوال الضد الأول، فلو كان زوال الضد الأول معللا بطريان الضد الثاني لزم توقف كل واحد منهما على الآخر،
وهو محال.
فهذه الوجوه الثلاثة دالة على المنع من صحة الموازنة.
The Thirty-Sixth Question. We say: The doctrine of balancing (al-muwāzanah) is false, and there are proofs for it: First: The prior (al-sābiq) either prevents the subsequent (al-ṭāri') from coming into existence, or does not prevent it. If it prevents it, the subsequent does not come into existence, and if the subsequent does not exist, the prior remains as it was, and this prevents nullification (al-iḥbāṭ). But if we say that the prior did not prevent the subsequent from coming into existence, rather it came into existence, then this subsequent either affects the non-existence of the prior or does not affect it. If it affects it, then the subsequent becomes predominant (ghālib) and the prior becomes overcome (maghlūb), and when the prior is overcome, it is impossible that it affect the annihilation of the subsequent, so balancing does not occur. If we say that the subsequent after coming into existence did not affect the annihilation of the prior, then the subsequent has existed with the prior and they have come together in existence, and on this estimation there is no opposition between them at all. If that is so, it is impossible that one of them affect the non-existence or removal of the other. So by this strong proof the falsity of the doctrine of balancing is established. The second proof of its falsity: If each of the prior and the subsequent were to affect the non-existence of the other, then their mutual influence would be either simultaneous or sequential, and both divisions are false, so the doctrine that each affects the non-existence of the other is false. We say that simultaneous occurrence is impossible because the cause of non-existence of each is the existence of the other, so if both non-existences occurred together, then both existences would occur together at the time of the non-existences, because the cause must exist when its effect exists; so they would be existent at the time they are non-existent, which is absurd. And sequential occurrence is impossible because it requires that the overcome becomes predominant after being overcome, which is absurd. The third proof: The condition for the occurrence of one of two opposites is the removal of the first opposite. So if the removal of the first opposite were caused by the occurrence of the second, then each would depend on the other, which is absurd. These three proofs indicate the prohibition of the correctness of balancing.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage presents three logical arguments against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of muwāzanah (balancing), which claims that a believer's reward and punishment cancel each other out in a balancing manner. The first argument explores two possibilities: either the prior good deed prevents the subsequent sin from existing (in which case no balancing occurs), or it does not prevent it. If it does not, then either the sin cancels the good deed (making the sin dominant), or it does not. If it cancels, the good deed cannot then cancel the sin, so balancing fails. If it does not cancel, both coexist without opposition, so no cancellation. The second argument examines mutual cancellation: if each cancels the other, it must be simultaneous or sequential. Simultaneous cancellation is impossible because it would require both to exist at the moment of their non-existence (contradiction). Sequential cancellation is impossible because it would require the overcome to become dominant again, which is absurd. The third argument uses the nature of opposites: the occurrence of a new opposite requires the prior removal of the first; if that removal is caused by the new opposite, then each depends on the other, which is circular. Thus all three proofs show the doctrine is false.

Key Terms
سابق
طارئ
إحباط
غالب
مغلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if a sin follows a good deed, they simply cancel each other out in a balanced way.
Correction: Razi's first proof shows that this balance is logically impossible: either the good prevents the sin (so no sin to cancel), or the sin cancels the good (so the good cannot cancel the sin), or both coexist with no effect—none of which results in mutual cancellation.

Checkpoint
In the first proof, what happens if the prior (sābiq) prevents the subsequent (ṭāri') from existing?
A) The subsequent cancels the prior.
B) No balancing occurs.
C) Balancing occurs.
D) The prior is cancelled.

Key Takeaway
The first proof exhaustively shows that mutual cancellation between a prior good deed and subsequent sin is logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin after a good deed, do you assume the sin 'cancels' the good or that the good 'balances' the sin? This proof shows such logic is impossible. What does that mean for how you view each deed's weight on your scale?
?
If no good deed can be nullified by a later sin, and no sin can be automatically cancelled by a prior good, what does that demand of you regarding your repentance for each sin—even those you commit after acts of worship?
?
The proof forces you to recognize that both good and bad deeds coexist and each has its own reality. Are you treating your sins as isolated incidents or do you see them as separate entries that must be individually addressed? How will this change your daily accountability?

The Second Proof: Simultaneous or Sequential Cancellation
Razi presents the second proof against muwāzanah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second logical proof against muwāzanah, which shows that mutual cancellation between prior and subsequent cannot occur either simultaneously or sequentially.

Core Idea
Mutual cancellation cannot be simultaneous or sequential—both lead to absurdity.

The second proof examines the claim that both the prior and subsequent cancel each other. Razi argues that such mutual influence must happen either at the same time or one after the other. He then demonstrates that both options are logically impossible. Let's see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic and translation. Pay attention to the two possibilities: simultaneous or sequential.
This is another case division.
2
Understand simultaneous cancellation
If each cancels the other simultaneously, then both non-existences occur at the same time. But the cause of each non-existence is the other's existence. So for both to be non-existent, both must be existent at that moment—a contradiction.
This is a classic argument against simultaneous mutual causation.
3
Understand sequential cancellation
If they cancel each other in sequence, then one becomes non-existent first, then the other. But after being overcome, the first would have to become predominant again to cancel the second, which is absurd.
The sequential option would require a reversal of roles, which is impossible.
4
Conclude
Both possibilities are false, so the doctrine that each cancels the other is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

How must you change your mindset to stop attributing events to conflicting causes and instead trust in God's single decree?
?
The proof demonstrates that mutual cancellation leads to absurdity—either contradiction or impossibility. Think of a situation where you tried to balance two competing desires or duties, hoping they would neutralize each other. In light of this proof, how can you commit to following God's command consistently, without trying to 'cancel out' one obligation with another?

The Third Proof: The Condition of Opposites
Razi presents the third proof against muwāzanah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof against muwāzanah, which uses the principle that the occurrence of a new opposite requires the prior removal of the first opposite, leading to a circular dependency.

Core Idea
The removal of the first opposite cannot be caused by the occurrence of the second opposite—that would be circular.

The third and final proof relies on the nature of opposites. When one opposite arises, the prior opposite must have already been removed. If we claim that the removal of the first is caused by the arrival of the second, then each depends on the other, which is absurd. This elegantly refutes any balancing of opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the short Arabic passage and translation. Understand the principle: the occurrence of a new opposite requires the prior removal of the first opposite.
This is a basic principle in kalam regarding opposites.
2
Apply to muwāzanah
If the removal of the first opposite is caused by the occurrence of the second, then the second's occurrence depends on the first's removal, and the first's removal depends on the second's occurrence—circular dependency.
3
Recognize the absurdity
Circular dependency is logically impossible, so muwāzanah is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السادسة والثلاثون
فنقول : القول بالموازنة باطل، ويدل عليه وجوه :
الأول: أن ثبوت السابق إما أن يمنع الطارئ من الدخول في الوجود، أو لا يمنعه، فإن منعه لم يدخل الطارئ في الوجود، و إذا لم يوجد الطارئ، بقي السابق كما كان، وهذا يمنع من الإحباط. وأما إن قلنا : إن السابق لم يمنع الطارئ من الدخول في الوجود، بل دخل في الوجود، فهذا الطارئ إما أن يؤثر في عدم السابق أو لا يؤثر فيه، فإن أثر فيه صار الطارئ غالبًا والسابق مغلوبًا، وعند صيرورة السابق مغلوبا امتنع أن يؤثر في إعدام الطارئ، فلم تكن الموازنة حاصلة، و إن قلنا بأن الطارئ بعد دخوله في الوجود لم يؤثر في إعدام السابق، فعلى هذا فقد وجد الطارئ مع السابق واجتمعا في الوجود، وعلى هذا التقدير لم يحصل بينهما منافاة أصلا، وإذا كان كذلك امتنع أن يكون أحدهما مؤثرًا في عدم الآخر، وفي زواله، فثبت بهذا البرهان القوي فساد القول بالموازنة.
الوجه الثاني في بطلانه هو أنه لو كان كل واحد من السابق والطارئ، مؤثرًا في عدم الآخر لكان تأثير كل واحد منهما في الآخر إما أن يكون معا، أو على التعاقب، والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بكون كل واحد منهما مؤثرا في عدم الآخر. وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع حصول ذلك معاً ؛ لأن المؤثر في عدم كل واحد منهما وجود الآخر، فلو حصل العدمان معا لحصل الوجودان معا حال حصول العدمين ؛ لأن العلة واجبة الحصول عند حصول المعلول فيلزم كونهما موجودين حال كونهما معدومين، وهو محال .
وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع حصول ذلك على التعاقب لا معا؛ لأنه يقتضي أن يعود المغلوب بعد صيرورته مغلوبًا غالبًا، وذلك محال.
الوجه الثالث : وهو أن شرط طريان أحد الضدين زوال الضد الأول، فلو كان زوال الضد الأول معللا بطريان الضد الثاني لزم توقف كل واحد منهما على الآخر،
وهو محال.
فهذه الوجوه الثلاثة دالة على المنع من صحة الموازنة.
The Thirty-Sixth Question. We say: The doctrine of balancing (al-muwāzanah) is false, and there are proofs for it: First: The prior (al-sābiq) either prevents the subsequent (al-ṭāri') from coming into existence, or does not prevent it. If it prevents it, the subsequent does not come into existence, and if the subsequent does not exist, the prior remains as it was, and this prevents nullification (al-iḥbāṭ). But if we say that the prior did not prevent the subsequent from coming into existence, rather it came into existence, then this subsequent either affects the non-existence of the prior or does not affect it. If it affects it, then the subsequent becomes predominant (ghālib) and the prior becomes overcome (maghlūb), and when the prior is overcome, it is impossible that it affect the annihilation of the subsequent, so balancing does not occur. If we say that the subsequent after coming into existence did not affect the annihilation of the prior, then the subsequent has existed with the prior and they have come together in existence, and on this estimation there is no opposition between them at all. If that is so, it is impossible that one of them affect the non-existence or removal of the other. So by this strong proof the falsity of the doctrine of balancing is established. The second proof of its falsity: If each of the prior and the subsequent were to affect the non-existence of the other, then their mutual influence would be either simultaneous or sequential, and both divisions are false, so the doctrine that each affects the non-existence of the other is false. We say that simultaneous occurrence is impossible because the cause of non-existence of each is the existence of the other, so if both non-existences occurred together, then both existences would occur together at the time of the non-existences, because the cause must exist when its effect exists; so they would be existent at the time they are non-existent, which is absurd. And sequential occurrence is impossible because it requires that the overcome becomes predominant after being overcome, which is absurd. The third proof: The condition for the occurrence of one of two opposites is the removal of the first opposite. So if the removal of the first opposite were caused by the occurrence of the second, then each would depend on the other, which is absurd. These three proofs indicate the prohibition of the correctness of balancing.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage presents three logical arguments against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of muwāzanah (balancing), which claims that a believer's reward and punishment cancel each other out in a balancing manner. The first argument explores two possibilities: either the prior good deed prevents the subsequent sin from existing (in which case no balancing occurs), or it does not prevent it. If it does not, then either the sin cancels the good deed (making the sin dominant), or it does not. If it cancels, the good deed cannot then cancel the sin, so balancing fails. If it does not cancel, both coexist without opposition, so no cancellation. The second argument examines mutual cancellation: if each cancels the other, it must be simultaneous or sequential. Simultaneous cancellation is impossible because it would require both to exist at the moment of their non-existence (contradiction). Sequential cancellation is impossible because it would require the overcome to become dominant again, which is absurd. The third argument uses the nature of opposites: the occurrence of a new opposite requires the prior removal of the first; if that removal is caused by the new opposite, then each depends on the other, which is circular. Thus all three proofs show the doctrine is false.

Key Terms
ضد
شرط
طريان
زوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that opposites can cause each other's removal.
Correction: Razi shows that the removal of the first opposite is a condition, not an effect, of the occurrence of the second. Claiming causation leads to circularity.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what does the occurrence of a new opposite require?
A) The existence of the prior opposite.
B) The removal of the prior opposite.
C) The simultaneous existence of both opposites.
D) The balancing of both opposites.

Key Takeaway
The third proof refutes muwāzanah by showing that the removal of the prior opposite cannot be caused by the occurrence of the subsequent opposite, as that leads to circular dependency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, when one situation ends and a new one begins, do you assume that the ending was automatically caused by the beginning? Reflect on a recent change you experienced. Can you identify whether the removal of the previous state was simply a condition or actually a cause? How might confusing the two lead you to misunderstand Allah’s decree?
?
This proof shows that circular dependency is absurd. Are there beliefs or habits you hold where you justify something by saying 'it’s just the way things are' without examining the cause? Commit to identifying one area of your life where you might be assuming a mutual causation that is actually circular, and resolve to break that pattern with proper reasoning.
?
If the removal of a prior opposite cannot be caused by the arrival of the next, then who is the true cause of every change? How does this affect your reliance on Allah in transitions? Write down one specific transition you are currently facing, and consciously attribute both the removal and the arrival to Allah, not to natural cycles.

Refutation of Nullification Without Balancing
Razi refutes Abu Ali's nullification without balancing using two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two arguments against Abu Ali's doctrine of nullification without balancing.

Core Idea
Nullification without balancing leads to absurdity and contradicts the Quran.

Razi now tackles the more extreme version of nullification: the view that a single sin can wipe out a lifetime of good deeds with no compensation. This view, held by Abu Ali al-Jubbai, is refuted with both rational and scriptural arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi presents two clearly numbered reasons.
2
Identify the first proof
The first proof is a reductio ad absurdum: if one sin nullifies all previous good deeds, then a lifelong worshipper who commits one sin equals someone who never worshipped. This is intuitively false in the 'first principles of intellects'.
Think about why this is a powerful argument from reason alone.
3
Identify the second proof
The second proof is scriptural: Quran 99:7 promises that every atom's weight of good will be seen. Nullification without balancing would mean good deeds are entirely lost, contradicting this verse.
Note how Razi uses a direct Quranic proof against the doctrine.
4
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that both forms of nullification (with and without balancing) are false, so nullification itself is false.
This shows the structure of a proper refutation: show that all possible versions of a view are false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القول الثاني: - وهو قول أبي علي وهو إثبات القول بالإحباط مع القول بعدم الموازنة - فهذا أيضًا باطل لوجهين : الأول : أن هذا يقتضي ان من عبد الله تعالى من أول عمره إلى آخره بأعظم الطاعات ثم شرب جرعة خمر أن يكون حاله وحال من لم يعبد الله قط على السوية؛ لأن عقاب شرب هذه الجرعة أبطل ثواب جميع تلك الطاعات، ولم يسقط البتة من عقاب هذا الشرب شيء، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل في بدائه العقول. والثاني : قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ﴾ [الزلزلة : ٧] فإذا كان عقاب الفسق احبط ثواب الطاعات المتقدمة، ولم يحبط بسبب ثواب تلك الطاعات شيء من عقاب هذه المعصية فقد ضاعت تلك الخيرات بالكلية، وذلك يناقض قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ﴾ فثبت أنه لو صح القول بالإحباط لكان إما مع الموازنة أو لا مع الموازنة، وثبت فساد القسمين، فوجب القول بفساد الإحباط أيضًا.
As for the second view—which is the view of Abū ʿAlī, namely establishing the doctrine of nullification (al-iḥbāṭ) while rejecting balancing—this too is false for two reasons. First: it implies that someone who worshipped God Almighty from the beginning of his life to the end with the greatest acts of obedience, then drank a sip of wine, would be in the same state as someone who never worshipped God at all; because the punishment for drinking this sip nullifies the reward of all those acts of obedience, and nothing of the punishment for this sip is reduced at all. It is known that this is false in the first principles of intellects. Second: His exalted saying: ﴾So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it﴿ [al-Zalzalah: 7]. If the punishment for sin nullified the reward of preceding acts of obedience, and nothing of the punishment for this sin is nullified due to the reward of those acts of obedience, then those good deeds are entirely lost, and that contradicts His saying: ﴾So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it.﴿ So it is established that if the doctrine of nullification were correct, it would be either with balancing or without balancing, and both divisions are false, so it is necessary to hold the falsity of nullification as well.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage refutes the view of Abū ʿAlī (al-Jubbā'ī) who held that a grave sin can nullify all previous good deeds without any balancing (muwāzanah) — i.e., the sin cancels all reward, but the reward does not reduce the punishment. Razi gives two arguments. First, a reductio ad absurdum: if a lifetime of worship is wiped out by a single sin, then the lifelong worshipper and the lifelong sinner would be equal in ultimate outcome, which is intuitively absurd. Second, a Quranic proof: the verse 'whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' guarantees that no good deed is ever wasted. If nullification (iḥbāṭ) were true without balancing, then good deeds would be entirely lost, contradicting the verse. Thus, since both forms of nullification (with and without balancing) are false, nullification itself is false.

Key Terms
إحباط
أبو علي
موازنة
بدائه العقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that nullification without balancing is more merciful than balancing, because it reduces the overall punishment.
Correction: Razi shows it leads to equalizing the righteous and the sinful, which is unjust and contradicts the Quran's promise that every good deed is seen.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's second proof against Abū ʿAlī's view, which Quranic verse does nullification without balancing contradict?
A) "And whoever does an atom's weight of evil will see it."
B) "So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it."
C) "Every soul will be repaid for what it has earned."
D) "That day the people will proceed in separate groups to be shown their deeds."

Key Takeaway
Nullification without balancing is false because it contradicts both rational intuition and the Quranic guarantee that no good deed is wasted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that a single sin can erase all your good deeds, making you feel hopeless? How does Razi's argument about the atom's weight of good change your perspective on that feeling? Will you now hold onto even the smallest good deed as something precious before Allah?
?
If you truly believe that no good deed is ever lost, how should that affect your daily choices—especially when you are tempted to neglect small acts of good? What is one small good deed you can commit to never let go of, starting today?
?
Razi shows that nullification without balancing is absurd because it would equate a lifelong worshipper who sins once with a lifelong sinner. Are there people in your life you have judged as 'hopeless' because of their sins? How does this lesson call you to reconsider—not only for others but for yourself—knowing that every atom's worth of good remains?
