The Proportional Argument Against Nullification (Ihbāṭ)
Razi presents a proportional argument against nullification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's proportional argument demonstrating that subsequent sin cannot nullify prior reward without leading to logical absurdity.

Core Idea
Nullifying only part of equal reward requires an arbitrary preference, which is rationally impossible.

Razi strengthens his case against nullification (ihbāṭ) with a detailed logical argument using a proportional example. He shows that even if we allow partial nullification, we cannot explain why one equal part of reward is cancelled over another without a reason. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Pay attention to the structure of the argument.
The argument uses a hypothetical scenario with 20 units of reward and 10 units of punishment.
2
Identify the two main options and their flaws
Notice that Razi presents two possibilities: full nullification (10 cancels 20) rejected as unjust, and partial nullification (10 cancels 10) which he then critiques.
The second option is the proponents' view, but Razi shows it implies an arbitrary preference.
3
Understand the principle of 'tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ'
Razi argues that the 10 units of punishment are equally related to both tens of reward. Cancelling one but not the other would be a preference without a preferring factor, which is rationally impossible.
This principle is central to kalam reasoning against arbitrary distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على فساد الإحباط : أنه إذا كان مستحقا لعشرين جزءا من الثواب ثم أتى بمعصية استحق بها عشرة أجزاء من العقاب فلو قلنا بأن هذا الطارئ يحبط السابق لكان إما أن تحبط هذه العشرة الطارئة تلك العشرين المتقدمة، وهذا قول باطل بالإجماع ؛ لأنه يكون ظلماً، وإما أن تكون العشرة الطارئة تحبط من العشرين المتقدم عشرة فقط - وهذا مذهب القائلين بالإحباط - ونقول : هذا محال؛ لأن نسبة هذه العشرة الطارئة إلى كل واحد من العشرتين السابقتين على السوية، فإذا اقتضت هذه العشرة الطارئة إزالة إحدى العشرتين السابقتين دون العشرة الأخرى لكان هذا ترجيحا لأحد طرفي الممكن المساوي على الآخر من غير مرجح، وهو محال، ولما بطل هذا القسم لم يبق إلا أن يقال : هذه العشرة الطارئة تؤثر في إزالة كل واحد من العشرتين المتقدمتين - وذلك هو القسم الأول، و قد أبطلناه ـ أو لا تؤثر في إزالة شيء مما وجد قبل ذلك؟ - وهذا يمنع من القول بالإحباط، وهو المطلوب - فثبت بمجموع ما ذكرناه أن الفسق الطارئ لا يزيل الثواب المتقدم.
Among what indicates the falsity of nullification (al-iḥbāṭ) is that if someone is entitled to twenty units of reward, then commits a sin through which he becomes entitled to ten units of punishment, then if we say that this subsequent nullifies the prior, then either these ten subsequent units nullify the twenty prior units—this is false by consensus because it would be injustice—or the ten subsequent units nullify only ten of the twenty prior units—and this is the doctrine of the proponents of nullification—and we say: this is impossible, because the ratio of these ten subsequent units to each of the two preceding tens is equal. So if these ten subsequent units cause the removal of one of the two preceding tens and not the other, this would be a preference of one side of a possible equal over the other without a preferring factor, which is impossible. And since this division is false, nothing remains except to say: these ten subsequent units affect the removal of each of the two preceding tens—and that is the first division, which we have falsified—or they do not affect the removal of anything that existed before?—and this prevents the doctrine of nullification, which is what is sought. So it is established by all that we have mentioned that subsequent sin does not remove prior reward.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed logical argument against nullification (iḥbāṭ) using a proportional example. Suppose a person has 20 units of reward and then commits a sin warranting 10 units of punishment. If the sin nullifies the reward, there are two possibilities: either the 10 units of sin cancel all 20 units of reward (which is unjust, as the sin is less), or they cancel only 10 units (keeping 10 reward). The second option is the proponents' view, but Razi shows it is impossible because the 10 units of punishment are equally related to both tens of reward. Cancelling only one of them without a reason would be an arbitrary preference (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ), which is rationally impossible. Thus the only remaining options are that the sin cancels all reward (already rejected) or cancels none. Since cancelling none contradicts nullification, nullification is false. Therefore, a subsequent sin does not erase prior reward.

Key Terms
إحباط
ثواب
عقاب
ترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think partial nullification is a reasonable compromise: the sin cancels an equal amount of reward, leaving the rest intact.
Correction: Razi shows that partial nullification leads to arbitrary preference because the sin is equally related to all parts of the reward. Without a reason to single out one part, the argument fails.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say that nullifying only 10 units of reward from 20 is impossible?
A) Because it would be unjust to nullify any reward.
B) Because the 10 units of punishment are equally related to both tens of reward, so singling one out requires an arbitrary preference.
C) Because the Quran prohibits partial nullification.
D) Because the sin is smaller than the reward.

Key Takeaway
A subsequent sin of lesser magnitude cannot nullify prior reward because any differential nullification of equal portions would be arbitrary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever believed that one good deed could be wiped out by a later sin, and if so, how does this argument challenge that assumption? How will you now view the permanence of your good actions?
?
If you were to consider your own scale of good and bad deeds, do you trust that Allah's justice is perfect and that no act is arbitrarily canceled? What does this rational proof teach you about trusting His justice even when you sin?
?
The argument relies on the principle of equal treatment. In your life, where do you need to apply this principle of fairness without arbitrary preference? How can you be more just in your judgments of others?

The Fate of the Sinful Believer and the Mu'tazila's First Proof
Razi states the Ash'ari view on the sinful believer and introduces the Mu'tazila's first proof for eternal punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on the fate of the sinful believer (fāsiq) and the first Mu'tazilite argument for eternal punishment.

Core Idea
Sinful believers may face temporary punishment before entering paradise; Mu'tazila argue sin nullifies reward.

This lesson presents the Ash'ari conclusion on the fate of the sinful believer after establishing that sin does not nullify prior reward. Razi then introduces the first Mu'tazilite proof that the sinner deserves eternal punishment, which he will later refute. Pay attention to the logical structure: two possible sequences are considered, one rejected by consensus, leading to the accepted view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation carefully. Notice that the passage cuts off mid-sentence at the end.
The passage begins mid-argument ('If it is established'). Assume the previous discussion has established that sin does not nullify reward.
2
Identify the Ash'ari reasoning
Razi presents two possibilities: either the sinner deserves no punishment, or he deserves punishment that does not remove his deserving reward. Then two delivery sequences are considered: reward first then punishment (rejected by consensus), or punishment first then reward (accepted).
The key point is that the sinful believer will eventually enter paradise after being punished for his sin.
3
Notice the beginning of the Mu'tazila proof
The Mu'tazila argue that the sinner deserves punishment, and this deserving of punishment nullifies the prior deserving of reward. This implies eternal punishment (khulūd). The passage cuts off here, but later Razi will refute this.
The term 'shubha' indicates this is a specious argument that will be answered.
4
Reflect on the structure
Consider why Razi first states the Ash'ari view before presenting the opponent's proof. This shows the argumentative style: establishing one's own position, then engaging with objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ثبت فنقول : ثبت أن المؤمن إذا أتى بالفسق فهو إما ان لا يستحق على فسقه عقابًا أو أن يستحق عليه عقاباً، لكن هذا الاستحقاق لا يزيل ما كان ثابتا قبل من استحقاق الثواب، وإذا ثبت هذا وجب إيصال هذا الثواب والعقاب إليه، فإما ان يوصل إليه ثواب إيمانه في الجنة ثم ينقل إلى النار، وهو باطل بالإجماع، وإما أن يوصل عقاب فسقه إليه في النار ثم ينقل إلى الجنة، وذلك هو المطلوب. أما المعتزلة فقد احتجوا على الخلود بوجوه : الشبهة الأولى : أن الفاسق بفسقه يستحق العقاب واستحقاق العقاب يبطل ما
Since it is established, we say: It is established that when a believer commits sin, either he does not deserve punishment for his sin, or he does deserve punishment, but this deserving does not remove what was previously established of deserving reward. And when this is established, it is necessary to deliver this reward and punishment to him. Either his reward for faith is delivered to him in paradise, then he is transferred to hell—this is false by consensus—or his punishment for sin is delivered to him in hell, then he is transferred to paradise—and that is what is sought. As for the Muʿtazila, they have argued for eternal (khulūd) [punishment] with proofs: The first doubt: that the sinner (al-fāsiq) deserves punishment by his sin, and the deserving of punishment nullifies what [previously existed of deserving reward].
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage presents the Ashʿarī position on the fate of a sinful believer (fāsiq) who dies without repentance. After establishing that sin does not nullify prior reward (from previous arguments), Razi states that the believer must receive both his reward and punishment. The only viable option is that he is punished in hell first (for his sin) and then transferred to paradise (for his faith), as the alternative—reward first then punishment—is unanimously rejected. Then Razi begins to present the Muʿtazilite arguments for eternal punishment (khulūd). The first proof (shubha) they offer is that the sinner deserves punishment, and this deserving of punishment nullifies his previous deserving of reward, implying eternal hell. The text cuts off mid-sentence, but it is clear that Razi is setting up the Muʿtazilite position to be refuted later.

Key Terms
فسق
خلود
ثواب
عقاب
إحباط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari view is that all believers, including sinful ones, go directly to paradise without any punishment.
Correction: The Ash'ari view, as presented by Razi, is that sinful believers may be punished temporarily in hell before entering paradise, based on the consensus that reward first then punishment is invalid.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the correct sequence for the sinful believer's reward and punishment?
A) Reward in paradise first, then punishment in hell.
B) Punishment in hell first, then reward in paradise.
C) No punishment, only reward.
D) Eternal punishment in hell.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view allows temporary punishment for sinful believers before entering paradise, while the Mu'tazila argue for eternal punishment based on sin nullifying reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your sins cannot destroy the reward for your iman? What sin in your life makes you doubt that, and how does this lesson challenge that doubt?
?
If you died tonight with a major sin you haven't repented from, do you trust that Allah will punish you briefly and then admit you to Paradise, or do you fear eternal punishment because of that sin? How does this teaching change your sense of urgency to repent?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that sin nullifies reward—do you sometimes feel that way about your own bad deeds, that they erase your good deeds? How does this passage give you hope or change your perspective?

The Absurdity of Equalizing the Obedient and Disobedient
Imam Razi presents two preliminary proofs against iḥbāṭ: a rational reductio and a scriptural proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite doctrine of iḥbāṭ leads to absurd conclusions that violate both reason and the Qur'an.

Core Idea
If sin annuls all good deeds, a lifetime of worship equals a life of sin—impossible.

Imam al-Razi begins his refutation by exposing a fatal flaw in the doctrine of iḥbāṭ. He shows that if a single sin could erase all previous acts of obedience, then a devout believer who commits one sin would end up no better than someone who never worshipped God at all. This absurdity is then reinforced by a clear Qur'anic proof from Surah al-Zalzalah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two distinct arguments: a rational reductio and a scriptural proof.
The first argument uses the example of a person who obeys then drinks wine.
2
Analyze the first argument
Identify the logical structure: if sin annuls all reward, the person's final state equals that of a non-believer. Ask yourself why this is impossible.
3
Connect with the scriptural argument
Observe how Imam Razi uses the Quranic verse to show that good deeds cannot be wasted. Understand how both arguments together refute iḥbāṭ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطاعات ثم شرب جرعة خمر أن يكون حاله وحال من لم يعبد الله قط على السوية؛ لأن عقاب شرب هذه الجرعة أبطل ثواب جميع تلك الطاعات، ولم يسقط البتة من عقاب هذا الشرب شيء، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل في بدائه العقول. والثاني : قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ﴾ فإذا كان عقاب الفسق أحبط ثواب الطاعات المتقدمة، ولم يحبط بسبب ثواب تلك الطاعات شيء من عقاب هذه المعصية فقد ضاعت تلك الخيرات بالكلية، وذلك يناقض قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ﴾ فثبت أنه لو صح القول بالإحباط لكان إما مع الموازنة أو لا مع الموازنة، وثبت فساد القسمين، فوجب القول بفساد الإحباط أيضًا. ومما يدل على فساد الإحباط : أنه إذا كان مستحقا لعشرين جزءا من الثواب ثم أتى بمعصية استحق بها عشرة أجزاء من العقاب فلو قلنا بأن هذا الطارئ يحبط السابق لكان إما أن تحبط هذه العشرة الطارئة تلك العشرين المتقدمة، وهذا قول باطل بالإجماع ؛ لأنه يكون ظلماً، وإما أن تكون العشرة الطارئة تحبط من العشرين المتقدم عشرة فقط - وهذا مذهب القائلين بالإحباط - ونقول : هذا محال؛ لأن نسبة هذه العشرة الطارئة إلى كل واحد من العشرتين السابقتين على السوية، فإذا اقتضت هذه العشرة الطارئة إزالة إحدى العشرتين السابقتين دون العشرة الأخرى لكان هذا ترجيحا لأحد طرفي الممكن المساوي على الآخر من غير مرجح، وهو محال، ولما بطل هذا القسم لم يبق إلا أن يقال : هذه العشرة الطارئة تؤثر في إزالة كل واحد من العشرتين المتقدمتين - وذلك هو القسم الأول، و قد أبطلناه ـ أو لا تؤثر في إزالة شيء مما وجد قبل ذلك؟ - وهذا يمنع من القول بالإحباط، وهو المطلوب - فثبت بمجموع ما ذكرناه أن الفسق الطارئ لا يزيل الثواب المتقدم. إذا ثبت فنقول : ثبت أن المؤمن إذا أتى بالفسق فهو إما أن لا يستحق على فسقه عقابًا أو أن يستحق عليه عقاباً، لكن هذا الاستحقاق لا يزيل ما كان ثابتا قبل من استحقاق الثواب، وإذا ثبت هذا وجب إيصال هذا الثواب والعقاب إليه، فإما أن يوصل إليه ثواب إيمانه في الجنة ثم ينقل إلى النار، وهو باطل بالإجماع، وإما أن يوصل عقاب فسقه إليه في النار ثم ينقل إلى الجنة، وذلك هو المطلوب.
Then consider [the case of] acts of obedience, then he drinks a gulp of wine, that his state and the state of one who never worshipped God at all would be equal, because the punishment for drinking this gulp annulled the reward of all those acts of obedience, and nothing of the punishment for this drink was dropped at all, and it is known that this is false in the first principles of intellects. The second: His statement, exalted: 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good shall see it.' So if the punishment for sin annulled the reward of previous acts of obedience, and due to the reward of those acts of obedience nothing is annulled of the punishment for this sin, then those good deeds are entirely wasted, and that contradicts His statement, exalted: 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good shall see it.' So it is established that if the doctrine of iḥbāṭ were true, it would either be with balancing or without balancing, and the falsity of both divisions is established, so it is necessary to assert the falsity of iḥbāṭ as well. And among what indicates the falsity of iḥbāṭ: that if [a person] is deserving of twenty parts of reward, then he commits a sin for which he deserves ten parts of punishment, then if we say that this occurring [sin] annuls the preceding [reward], it would either be that these ten occurring [parts] annul those twenty preceding ones — and this is a false statement by consensus because it would be injustice — or that the ten occurring parts annul from the twenty preceding only ten — and this is the doctrine of those who affirm iḥbāṭ — and we say: this is impossible, because the relation of these ten occurring parts to each of the two preceding tens is equal, so if these ten occurring parts necessitate the removal of one of the two preceding tens but not the other ten, this would be a preference of one of the two sides of an equally possible matter over the other without a preferrer, and that is impossible. And when this division is invalidated, nothing remains except to say: these ten occurring parts effect the removal of each of the two preceding tens — and that is the first division, and we have invalidated it — or they do not effect the removal of anything that existed before? — and this prevents the doctrine of iḥbāṭ, and that is what is sought. So it is established by the sum of what we have mentioned that the occurring sin does not remove the preceding reward. If this is established, we say: it is established that the believer when he commits sin, either he does not deserve punishment for his sin, or he deserves punishment for it, but this deserving does not remove what was previously established of deserving reward. And when this is established, it is necessary to convey this reward and punishment to him. So either his reward for his faith is conveyed to him in Paradise, then he is transferred to the Fire — and this is false by consensus — or his punishment for his sin is conveyed to him in the Fire, then he is transferred to Paradise, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a rational refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of iḥbāṭ (annulment of good deeds by sin). Imam al-Razi presents two main arguments. The first uses the example of a person who performed many acts of obedience and then commits a single sin. If the sin annuls all previous reward, his final state would equal that of a person who never worshipped God — an absurd conclusion rejected by clear reason. The second argument is a scriptural appeal: if sin annuls reward without any balancing, then all good deeds are wasted, contradicting the Qur'anic promise that every atom's weight of good will be seen. The author then presents a detailed logical proof using the example of twenty parts of reward and ten parts of punishment. He considers two possibilities: either the ten parts of punishment annul all twenty of reward (injustice) or they annul only ten (preference without a reason). Both are impossible. Therefore, the sin does not annul any reward. The conclusion is that the believer who commits sin still deserves reward for his faith, and the punishment for sin is separate. He will be punished in Hell and then transferred to Paradise, since transferring him first to Paradise and then to Hell is impossible by consensus.

Key Terms
إحباط (iḥbāṭ)
ثواب (thawāb)
عقاب (ʿiqāb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the first argument in this passage?
A) That punishment for sin can be greater than reward for obedience.
B) That if sin annuls all reward, the obedient and disobedient become equal, which is absurd.
C) That the Qur'an promises reward only for major acts of worship.
D) That sin cannot be punished because it would contradict God's mercy.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of iḥbāṭ is rationally absurd because it equalizes the obedient and the disobedient, and it contradicts the Qur'anic promise that every good deed will be rewarded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a single sin could annihilate all your good deeds, how would you live differently today? But since it doesn't, how does that affect the way you see your past sins and your present obedience?
?
Do you ever treat your good deeds as insignificant because you think your sins outweigh them? What does this lesson demand you change about that attitude?
?
Imagine standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment with the knowledge that every atom's weight of good will be seen. Are you building your life on that promise, or do you secretly despair that your sins have wiped it all away? What concrete step will you take today to act on that promise?

The Logical Proof with Twenty and Ten Parts
Imam Razi uses a detailed logical analysis of reward and punishment divisions to refute iḥbāṭ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed logical proof that demonstrates the impossibility of iḥbāṭ using the example of twenty parts of reward and ten parts of punishment.

Core Idea
If sin annuls reward, it must annul all or part—both options lead to impossibility.

Imam al-Razi now presents a more refined logical argument. He constructs a scenario where a person deserves twenty parts of reward and then commits a sin deserving ten parts of punishment. He examines every possible way the sin could affect the reward and shows that each leads to a contradiction. This proof is a classic example of kalam reasoning about tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ (preference without a preferrer).

Guided Path
1
Understand the example
Grasp the scenario: a person deserves 20 parts of reward, then commits a sin deserving 10 parts of punishment. List the logical possibilities for how the punishment could affect the reward.
There are three possibilities: annul all 20, annul only 10, or annul none.
2
Walk through each possibility
Follow the text as it rejects the first option (annulling all 20 as unjust) and the second (annulling only 10 as arbitrary preference without reason).
3
Recognize the logical conclusion
The third option (no annulment) is the only remaining one and it negates iḥbāṭ entirely. Understand why this proves that sin does not remove reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطاعات ثم شرب جرعة خمر أن يكون حاله وحال من لم يعبد الله قط على السوية؛ لأن عقاب شرب هذه الجرعة أبطل ثواب جميع تلك الطاعات، ولم يسقط البتة من عقاب هذا الشرب شيء، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل في بدائه العقول. والثاني : قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ﴾ فإذا كان عقاب الفسق أحبط ثواب الطاعات المتقدمة، ولم يحبط بسبب ثواب تلك الطاعات شيء من عقاب هذه المعصية فقد ضاعت تلك الخيرات بالكلية، وذلك يناقض قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ﴾ فثبت أنه لو صح القول بالإحباط لكان إما مع الموازنة أو لا مع الموازنة، وثبت فساد القسمين، فوجب القول بفساد الإحباط أيضًا. ومما يدل على فساد الإحباط : أنه إذا كان مستحقا لعشرين جزءا من الثواب ثم أتى بمعصية استحق بها عشرة أجزاء من العقاب فلو قلنا بأن هذا الطارئ يحبط السابق لكان إما أن تحبط هذه العشرة الطارئة تلك العشرين المتقدمة، وهذا قول باطل بالإجماع ؛ لأنه يكون ظلماً، وإما أن تكون العشرة الطارئة تحبط من العشرين المتقدم عشرة فقط - وهذا مذهب القائلين بالإحباط - ونقول : هذا محال؛ لأن نسبة هذه العشرة الطارئة إلى كل واحد من العشرتين السابقتين على السوية، فإذا اقتضت هذه العشرة الطارئة إزالة إحدى العشرتين السابقتين دون العشرة الأخرى لكان هذا ترجيحا لأحد طرفي الممكن المساوي على الآخر من غير مرجح، وهو محال، ولما بطل هذا القسم لم يبق إلا أن يقال : هذه العشرة الطارئة تؤثر في إزالة كل واحد من العشرتين المتقدمتين - وذلك هو القسم الأول، و قد أبطلناه ـ أو لا تؤثر في إزالة شيء مما وجد قبل ذلك؟ - وهذا يمنع من القول بالإحباط، وهو المطلوب - فثبت بمجموع ما ذكرناه أن الفسق الطارئ لا يزيل الثواب المتقدم. إذا ثبت فنقول : ثبت أن المؤمن إذا أتى بالفسق فهو إما أن لا يستحق على فسقه عقابًا أو أن يستحق عليه عقاباً، لكن هذا الاستحقاق لا يزيل ما كان ثابتا قبل من استحقاق الثواب، وإذا ثبت هذا وجب إيصال هذا الثواب والعقاب إليه، فإما أن يوصل إليه ثواب إيمانه في الجنة ثم ينقل إلى النار، وهو باطل بالإجماع، وإما أن يوصل عقاب فسقه إليه في النار ثم ينقل إلى الجنة، وذلك هو المطلوب.
Then consider [the case of] acts of obedience, then he drinks a gulp of wine, that his state and the state of one who never worshipped God at all would be equal, because the punishment for drinking this gulp annulled the reward of all those acts of obedience, and nothing of the punishment for this drink was dropped at all, and it is known that this is false in the first principles of intellects. The second: His statement, exalted: 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good shall see it.' So if the punishment for sin annulled the reward of previous acts of obedience, and due to the reward of those acts of obedience nothing is annulled of the punishment for this sin, then those good deeds are entirely wasted, and that contradicts His statement, exalted: 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good shall see it.' So it is established that if the doctrine of iḥbāṭ were true, it would either be with balancing or without balancing, and the falsity of both divisions is established, so it is necessary to assert the falsity of iḥbāṭ as well. And among what indicates the falsity of iḥbāṭ: that if [a person] is deserving of twenty parts of reward, then he commits a sin for which he deserves ten parts of punishment, then if we say that this occurring [sin] annuls the preceding [reward], it would either be that these ten occurring [parts] annul those twenty preceding ones — and this is a false statement by consensus because it would be injustice — or that the ten occurring parts annul from the twenty preceding only ten — and this is the doctrine of those who affirm iḥbāṭ — and we say: this is impossible, because the relation of these ten occurring parts to each of the two preceding tens is equal, so if these ten occurring parts necessitate the removal of one of the two preceding tens but not the other ten, this would be a preference of one of the two sides of an equally possible matter over the other without a preferrer, and that is impossible. And when this division is invalidated, nothing remains except to say: these ten occurring parts effect the removal of each of the two preceding tens — and that is the first division, and we have invalidated it — or they do not effect the removal of anything that existed before? — and this prevents the doctrine of iḥbāṭ, and that is what is sought. So it is established by the sum of what we have mentioned that the occurring sin does not remove the preceding reward. If this is established, we say: it is established that the believer when he commits sin, either he does not deserve punishment for his sin, or he deserves punishment for it, but this deserving does not remove what was previously established of deserving reward. And when this is established, it is necessary to convey this reward and punishment to him. Commentary
This passage is a rational refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of iḥbāṭ (annulment of good deeds by sin). Imam al-Razi presents two main arguments. The first uses the example of a person who performed many acts of obedience and then commits a single sin. If the sin annuls all previous reward, his final state would equal that of a person who never worshipped God — an absurd conclusion rejected by clear reason. The second argument is a scriptural appeal: if sin annuls reward without any balancing, then all good deeds are wasted, contradicting the Qur'anic promise that every atom's weight of good will be seen. The author then presents a detailed logical proof using the example of twenty parts of reward and ten parts of punishment. He considers two possibilities: either the ten parts of punishment annul all twenty of reward (injustice) or they annul only ten (preference without a reason). Both are impossible. Therefore, the sin does not annul any reward. The conclusion is that the believer who commits sin still deserves reward for his faith, and the punishment for sin is separate. He will be punished in Hell and then transferred to Paradise, since transferring him first to Paradise and then to Hell is impossible by consensus.

Key Terms
إحباط (iḥbāṭ)
ثواب (thawāb)
عقاب (ʿiqāb)
ترجيح بلا مرجح (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that iḥbāṭ means sin completely erases all good deeds equally.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that even partial annulment (annulling only some of the reward) is impossible because it would require arbitrary preference without reason, which is rationally untenable.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi reject the possibility that 10 parts of punishment annul exactly 10 out of 20 parts of reward?
A) Because it would be unjust.
B) Because it would require preferring one part over another without a reason.
C) Because the Quran forbids partial annulment.
D) Because the punishment is greater than the reward.

Key Takeaway
The logical proof using twenty and ten parts demonstrates that sin cannot annul any reward, because any form of annulment leads to either injustice or arbitrary preference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that a single sin erased years of good deeds? How does this logical proof refute that despair, and what does it mean for your hope in Allah's justice?
?
If you knew that Allah does not allow sin to arbitrarily cancel your good deeds, how would that change the way you approach repentance and the accumulation of good actions starting today?
?
This argument shows that God's justice is not arbitrary but perfectly precise. When you sin, do you truly believe that Allah weighs deeds with perfect fairness? What would it take for you to live with that conviction in your daily choices?

The Consequence: Punishment in Hell then Paradise
Imam Razi concludes the refutation by affirming the separate delivery of reward and punishment for the sinful believer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashʿarī position that a believer who commits sin deserves both reward and punishment, and that punishment will be administered in Hell before entry into Paradise.

Core Idea
A sinful believer deserves reward for faith and punishment for sin—both will be fulfilled, with punishment first.

Having established that sin does not annul reward, Imam al-Razi now draws the necessary conclusion. The believer who commits sin still deserves the reward for his faith, and also deserves punishment for his sin. Both must be delivered, but it is impossible to give reward in Paradise and then transfer to Hell—so the only coherent option is to punish in Hell first, then admit to Paradise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possibilities
After proving sin does not remove reward, Imam Razi states that the believer either deserves punishment or not. He affirms he does, and that punishment does not remove reward.
2
Analyze the delivery order
He then considers two orders: reward first then punishment (invalid by consensus), or punishment first then reward (the correct conclusion).
3
Understand the theological significance
This passage defends the orthodox view that believers who sin will be purified in Hell before entering Paradise, upholding both God's justice and mercy.

الطاعات ثم شرب جرعة خمر أن يكون حاله وحال من لم يعبد الله قط على السوية؛ لأن عقاب شرب هذه الجرعة أبطل ثواب جميع تلك الطاعات، ولم يسقط البتة من عقاب هذا الشرب شيء، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل في بدائه العقول. والثاني : قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ﴾ فإذا كان عقاب الفسق أحبط ثواب الطاعات المتقدمة، ولم يحبط بسبب ثواب تلك الطاعات شيء من عقاب هذه المعصية فقد ضاعت تلك الخيرات بالكلية، وذلك يناقض قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ﴾ فثبت أنه لو صح القول بالإحباط لكان إما مع الموازنة أو لا مع الموازنة، وثبت فساد القسمين، فوجب القول بفساد الإحباط أيضًا. ومما يدل على فساد الإحباط : أنه إذا كان مستحقا لعشرين جزءا من الثواب ثم أتى بمعصية استحق بها عشرة أجزاء من العقاب فلو قلنا بأن هذا الطارئ يحبط السابق لكان إما أن تحبط هذه العشرة الطارئة تلك العشرين المتقدمة، وهذا قول باطل بالإجماع ؛ لأنه يكون ظلماً، وإما أن تكون العشرة الطارئة تحبط من العشرين المتقدم عشرة فقط - وهذا مذهب القائلين بالإحباط - ونقول : هذا محال؛ لأن نسبة هذه العشرة الطارئة إلى كل واحد من العشرتين السابقتين على السوية، فإذا اقتضت هذه العشرة الطارئة إزالة إحدى العشرتين السابقتين دون العشرة الأخرى لكان هذا ترجيحا لأحد طرفي الممكن المساوي على الآخر من غير مرجح، وهو محال، ولما بطل هذا القسم لم يبق إلا أن يقال : هذه العشرة الطارئة تؤثر في إزالة كل واحد من العشرتين المتقدمتين - وذلك هو القسم الأول، و قد أبطلناه ـ أو لا تؤثر في إزالة شيء مما وجد قبل ذلك؟ - وهذا يمنع من القول بالإحباط، وهو المطلوب - فثبت بمجموع ما ذكرناه أن الفسق الطارئ لا يزيل الثواب المتقدم. إذا ثبت فنقول : ثبت أن المؤمن إذا أتى بالفسق فهو إما أن لا يستحق على فسقه عقابًا أو أن يستحق عليه عقاباً، لكن هذا الاستحقاق لا يزيل ما كان ثابتا قبل من استحقاق الثواب، وإذا ثبت هذا وجب إيصال هذا الثواب والعقاب إليه، فإما أن يوصل إليه ثواب إيمانه في الجنة ثم ينقل إلى النار، وهو باطل بالإجماع، وإما أن يوصل عقاب فسقه إليه في النار ثم ينقل إلى الجنة، وذلك هو المطلوب.
Then consider [the case of] acts of obedience, then he drinks a gulp of wine, that his state and the state of one who never worshipped God at all would be equal, because the punishment for drinking this gulp annulled the reward of all those acts of obedience, and nothing of the punishment for this drink was dropped at all, and it is known that this is false in the first principles of intellects. The second: His statement, exalted: 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good shall see it.' So if the punishment for sin annulled the reward of previous acts of obedience, and due to the reward of those acts of obedience nothing is annulled of the punishment for this sin, then those good deeds are entirely wasted, and that contradicts His statement, exalted: 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good shall see it.' So it is established that if the doctrine of iḥbāṭ were true, it would either be with balancing or without balancing, and the falsity of both divisions is established, so it is necessary to assert the falsity of iḥbāṭ as well. And among what indicates the falsity of iḥbāṭ: that if [a person] is deserving of twenty parts of reward, then he commits a sin for which he deserves ten parts of punishment, then if we say that this occurring [sin] annuls the preceding [reward], it would either be that these ten occurring [parts] annul those twenty preceding ones — and this is a false statement by consensus because it would be injustice — or that the ten occurring parts annul from the twenty preceding only ten — and this is the doctrine of those who affirm iḥbāṭ — and we say: this is impossible, because the relation of these ten occurring parts to each of the two preceding tens is equal, so if these ten occurring parts necessitate the removal of one of the two preceding tens but not the other ten, this would be a preference of one of the two sides of an equally possible matter over the other without a preferrer, and that is impossible. And when this division is invalidated, nothing remains except to say: these ten occurring parts effect the removal of each of the two preceding tens — and that is the first division, and we have invalidated it — or they do not effect the removal of anything that existed before? — and this prevents the doctrine of iḥbāṭ, and that is what is sought. So it is established by the sum of what we have mentioned that the occurring sin does not remove the preceding reward. If this is established, we say: it is established that the believer when he commits sin, either he does not deserve punishment for his sin, or he deserves punishment for it, but this deserving does not remove what was previously established of deserving reward. And when this is established, it is necessary to convey this reward and punishment to him. So either his reward for his faith is conveyed to him in Paradise, then he is transferred to the Fire — and this is false by consensus — or his punishment for his sin is conveyed to him in the Fire, then he is transferred to Paradise, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a rational refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of iḥbāṭ (annulment of good deeds by sin). Imam al-Razi presents two main arguments. The first uses the example of a person who performed many acts of obedience and then commits a single sin. If the sin annuls all previous reward, his final state would equal that of a person who never worshipped God — an absurd conclusion rejected by clear reason. The second argument is a scriptural appeal: if sin annuls reward without any balancing, then all good deeds are wasted, contradicting the Qur'anic promise that every atom's weight of good will be seen. The author then presents a detailed logical proof using the example of twenty parts of reward and ten parts of punishment. He considers two possibilities: either the ten parts of punishment annul all twenty of reward (injustice) or they annul only ten (preference without a reason). Both are impossible. Therefore, the sin does not annul any reward. The conclusion is that the believer who commits sin still deserves reward for his faith, and the punishment for sin is separate. He will be punished in Hell and then transferred to Paradise, since transferring him first to Paradise and then to Hell is impossible by consensus.

Key Terms
إحباط (iḥbāṭ)
ثواب (thawāb)
عقاب (ʿiqāb)
جنة (jannah)
نار (nār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that any sin leads to eternal damnation, even for believers.
Correction: Imam Razi affirms that believers can enter Hell for a time due to sin, but their faith ensures eventual entry into Paradise. Sin does not nullify the reward of faith.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the correct order of delivering reward and punishment to a sinful believer?
A) Punishment first in Hell, then reward in Paradise.
B) Reward first in Paradise, then punishment in Hell.
C) Only reward, because punishment is cancelled by faith.
D) Neither, because the believer will not be punished.

Key Takeaway
The sinful believer deserves both reward and punishment; they are fulfilled by punishing in Hell first, then granting Paradise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that your deeds of faith still merit reward, or do you let guilt convince you that your good deeds are wiped away? How does this passage challenge your inner narrative about your worth before Allah? Write one sin you have been despairing over and recite the line 'this deserving does not remove what was previously established of deserving reward' as an affirmation of hope.
?
If you knew that every unrepented sin will be punished—even if temporarily—would you treat minor sins as lightly as you do? What specific habit do you need to change today because you now understand that punishment is real and measured? Commit to one concrete action to break that habit within the next 24 hours.
?
The passage concludes that punishment comes before Paradise for the sinful believer. Are you living as though you expect to be purified through hardship before ease? Or do you resent difficulties, forgetting that they may be the very Fire that refines you for Paradise? How can you welcome a trial this week as a means of purification rather than a sign of Allah's anger?

Divine Decree

First Mu'tazilite Doubt and the Ash'ari Response
Imam Razi presents the first Mu'tazilite doubt on eternal punishment and the first aspect of the Ash'ari answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazilite argument for eternal punishment based on the incompatibility of reward and punishment, and the Ash'ari response that God is not obligated to reward or punish.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari response to iḥbāṭ is to deny that God owes anything to anyone.

The Mu'tazila argued that after sin, a person loses all deserving of reward, making eternal punishment necessary. This lesson presents their key proof and the beginning of Imam al-Razi's Ash'ari rebuttal, which undercuts the entire argument by rejecting the notion that God is bound by human concepts of desert.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite argument
Carefully read the passage where the Mu'tazila present their first doubt. Notice the reasoning: punishment is pure harm, reward is pure benefit, they cannot coexist, so deserving punishment annuls deserving reward.
This is known as iḥbāṭ (annulment of reward).
2
Examine the proof from Paradise
See how they argue that the sinner cannot enter Paradise either by desert (since reward is gone) or by favor (since Paradise is only for reward), citing Quran 23:1-11.
3
Understand the Ash'ari response
Read Imam al-Razi's first response: the argument assumes God is obligated to reward the obedient and punish the sinner, but the Ash'ari position is that God is not obliged to do anything.
This is a fundamental theological difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المعتزلة فقد احتجوا على الخلود بوجوه : الشبهة الأولى : أن الفاسق بفسقه يستحق العقاب واستحقاق العقاب يبطل ما كان ثابتا قبل ذلك من استحقاق الثواب، وذلك لأن العقاب مضرة خالصة دائمة، والثواب منفعة خالصة دائمة، والجمع بينهما محال، فكان الجمع بين استحقاقيهما محالاً، وإذا ثبت استحقاق العقاب وجب أن يزول استحقاق الثواب . وهذا هو العمدة الكبرى لهم في إثبات القول بالإحباط إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو نقل الفاسق من النار إلى الجنة، لكان دخول الجنة . إما أن يكون بالاستحقاق أو بالتفضل والأول باطل. لما بينا أن هذا الاستحقاق غير حاصل. والثاني أيضًا باطل لأن الجنة دار الثواب، لا دار التفضل . والدليل عليه : قوله تعالى : ﴿قَدْ أَفْلَحَ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ الَّذِينَ هُمْ فِي صَلَاتِهِمْ خَشِعُونَ وَالَّذِينَ هُمْ عَنِ اللَّغْوِ مُعْرِضُونَ وَالَّذِينَ هُمْ لِلزَّكَاةِ فَاعِلُونَ وَالَّذِينَ هُمْ لِفُرُوجِهِمْ حَافِظُونَ إِلَّا عَلَى أَزْوَاجِهِمْ أَوْ مَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَانُهُمْ فَإِنَّهُمْ غَيْرُ مَلُومِينَ فَمَنِ ابْتَغَىٰ وَرَاءَ ذَٰلِكَ فَأُولَٰئِكَ هُمُ الْعَادُونَ وَالَّذِينَ هُمْ لِأَمَانَاتِهِمْ وَعَهْدِهِمْ رَاعُونَ وَالَّذِينَ هُمْ عَلَىٰ صَلَوَاتِهِمْ يُحَافِظُونَ أُولَٰئِكَ هُمُ الْوَارِثُونَ الَّذِينَ يَرِثُونَ الْفِرْدَوْسَ﴾ [المؤمنون: ١-١١]. الشبهة الثانية: تمسكوا بالآيات المشتملة على لفظ الخلود في وعيد الفساد. كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَن يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِنًا مُتَعَمِّدًا فَجَزَاؤُهُ جَهَنَّمُ خَالِدًا فِيهَا﴾ [النساء: ٩٣] قالوا : الخلود هو الدوام. واحتجوا عليه بوجوه : أحدها قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا جَعَلْنَا لِبَشَرٍ مِّن قَبْلِكَ الْخُلْدَ﴾ [الأنبياء: ٣٤] ولا شك في أن من كان قبله قد جعل الله لهم لبثا طويلاً في الدنيا . فلو كان الخلود عبارة عن اللبث الطويل فقط لم يكن لهذه الآية معنى. وثانيها : إنه يصح تأكيده بلفظ التأييد قال تعالى : ﴿خَالِدِينَ فِيهَا أَبَدًا﴾ [النساء: ٥٧] ونص أهل اللغة على أن قوله «أبدًا» تأكيد لمعنى الخلود. فلولا أن لفظ الخلود يفيد الدوام، وإلا لم يصح تأكيده بما يفيد الدوام. وثالثها : إنه يصح أن يستثنى من الخلود أي مقدار أريد. فيقال : أنتم خالدون إلا كذا وكذا . والدليل عليه قوله : ﴿خَالِدِينَ فِيهَا مَا دَامَتِ السَّمَاوَاتُ وَالْأَرْضُ إِلَّا مَا شَاءَ رَبُّكَ﴾ [هود: ١٠٧]. الشبهة الثالثة: قوله تعالى في صفة الفجار : ﴿وَمَا هُمْ عَنْهَا بِغَائِبِينَ﴾ [الانفطار: ١٦] ولو خرجوا من النار لصاروا عنها غائبين . والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى من وجوه : الأول : إنها مبنية على أن المطيع يستحق بطاعته ثوابًا، والمذنب يستحق بذنبه عقابًا . ونحن لا نقول بذلك، فإنا لا نوجب على الله شيئًا.
As for the Muʿtazila, they have argued for eternal [punishment] by [several] points: The first doubt: that the sinner, by his sin, deserves punishment, and the deserving of punishment annuls what was previously established of deserving reward, because punishment is pure and lasting harm, and reward is pure and lasting benefit, and combining them is impossible, so combining their deservingness is impossible. And when the deserving of punishment is established, the deserving of reward must cease. And this is their major support in establishing the doctrine of iḥbāṭ. If this is established, we say: if the sinner were transferred from the Fire to Paradise, his entry into Paradise would either be by deserving or by favor. The first is false, because we have shown that this deserving does not occur. The second is also false, because Paradise is the abode of reward, not the abode of favor. The proof of that is His statement, exalted: 'Successful indeed are the believers, who are humble in their prayers, and who turn away from idle talk, and who are doers of zakah, and who guard their private parts except from their wives or those their right hands possess, for then they are not blameworthy, but whoever seeks beyond that, then those are the transgressors; and who are faithful to their trusts and their covenant, and who carefully maintain their prayers. These are the inheritors who will inherit Paradise.' (al-Mu'minun 1-11). The second doubt: They held to the verses that contain the word 'khulūd' in the threat against sin. Such as His statement: 'Whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding therein forever.' (al-Nisa 93). They said: 'khulūd' is perpetuity. They argued for this by [several] points: One of them is His statement: 'And We did not grant to any human before you eternity' (al-Anbiya 34). There is no doubt that those before him, God gave them a long stay in the world. So if 'khulūd' only meant a long stay, this verse would have no meaning. The second: that it is valid to emphasize it with the word 'ta'bid'. He said, exalted: 'abiding therein forever' (al-Nisa 57). And the scholars of language state that His word 'abadan' is an emphasis on the meaning of 'khulūd'. So if the word 'khulūd' did not indicate perpetuity, it would not be valid to emphasize it with what indicates perpetuity. The third: that it is valid to make an exception from 'khulūd' for any amount intended. So it is said: 'You are abiding except such and such.' The proof of that is His statement: 'Abiding therein as long as the heavens and the earth endure, except what your Lord wills.' (Hud 107). The third doubt: His statement, exalted, describing the wicked: 'And they are not absent from it' (al-Infitar 16). And if they left the Fire, they would become absent from it. The answer to the first doubt is by [several] aspects: The first: It is built upon the claim that the obedient person deserves reward for his obedience, and the sinner deserves punishment for his sin. And we do not say that, for we do not obligate anything upon God.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage presents three Mu'tazilite arguments for the eternal punishment of the grave sinner (fasiq) and begins the Ash'ari response to the first. The first argument is the major proof for iḥbāṭ: punishment is pure, lasting harm, and reward is pure, lasting benefit; they cannot be combined, so if punishment is deserved, reward must be annulled. From this, they argue that after punishment, the sinner cannot enter Paradise by either desert (since reward is gone) or divine favor (since Paradise is a place of reward, not favor), citing Qur'an 23:1-11. The second argument relies on verses using the word 'khulūd' (e.g., 4:93), claiming it means eternal duration. They support this with linguistic points: (a) the word is used for absolute eternity in 21:34, not just long stay; (b) it can be emphasized with 'abadan' (forever) in 4:57; (c) it allows exceptions, as in 11:107, showing it implies perpetuity unless specified. The third argument cites 82:16 that the wicked are 'not absent' from Hell, implying they never leave. Imam al-Razi then begins answering the first doubt. The first aspect of the response is to deny the premise that God is obligated to reward the obedient or punish the sinner. The Ash'ari position is that God does not owe anything to anyone; He may reward or punish as He wills. By rejecting human desert, the entire Mu'tazilite argument collapses. The answers to the second and third doubts are not provided in this passage.

Key Terms
خلود (khulūd)
إحباط (iḥbāṭ)
استحقاق (istiḥqāq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite argument for eternal punishment is logically airtight and cannot be refuted.
Correction: The argument depends on the assumption that God is obligated to reward/punish based on desert, which the Ash'aris reject. Once that premise is denied, the argument loses its force.

Checkpoint
What is the first aspect of Imam al-Razi's response to the first Mu'tazilite doubt regarding iḥbāṭ?
A) He argues that the sinner can be punished in this world.
B) He denies that God is obligated to reward the obedient or punish the sinner.
C) He affirms that the reward is preserved but not in Paradise.
D) He claims that the sinner will never enter Paradise.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite argument for eternal punishment collapses if one does not accept that God is obligated to reward or punish based on desert.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel entitled to God's reward for your good deeds? How does the truth that God owes you nothing reshape that feeling into pure gratitude?
?
If your salvation depends entirely on God's mercy and not on your own merit, how should that change the way you live today?
?
When you sin, do you despair of God's mercy because you feel you no longer deserve Paradise? How does this lesson free you from that despair while still calling you to obedience?

Second and Third Mu'tazilite Doubts — Linguistic and Scriptural Evidence
Imam Razi presents the second and third Mu'tazilite doubts based on language and Qur'anic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite linguistic arguments that the word 'khulūd' means eternal duration, and their use of the Qur'anic verse stating the wicked are never absent from Hell to support eternal punishment.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila used language and scripture to prove that 'khulūd' means forever, leaving no room for eventual salvation.

The Mu'tazila did not rely only on rational argument; they also advanced linguistic and scriptural proofs. This lesson presents two further arguments: one based on the necessary meaning of 'khulūd' and the other on a verse stating the wicked are never absent from Hell. Note that the Ash'ari responses to these arguments are not included in this passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the linguistic proofs for 'khulūd'
Examine the three linguistic points: (1) the word 'khulūd' in 21:34 refers to absolute eternity, not just a long stay; (2) it can be emphasized with 'abadan' (forever) in 4:57; (3) it can take exceptions, as in 11:107, implying it normally means permanence.
These points aim to show that 'khulūd' in the Qur'anic threats must be interpreted as eternal.
2
Analyze the third doubt
Read the short third doubt: Q. 82:16 says the wicked are 'not absent' from Hell, implying they never leave.
This is a direct scriptural citation.
3
Reflect on the overall Mu'tazilite position
Consider how these arguments complement the rational first doubt, building a cumulative case for eternal punishment.
The Ash'ari school would later offer counter-interpretations of these verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المعتزلة فقد احتجوا على الخلود بوجوه : الشبهة الأولى : أن الفاسق بفسقه يستحق العقاب واستحقاق العقاب يبطل ما كان ثابتا قبل ذلك من استحقاق الثواب، وذلك لأن العقاب مضرة خالصة دائمة، والثواب منفعة خالصة دائمة، والجمع بينهما محال، فكان الجمع بين استحقاقيهما محالاً، وإذا ثبت استحقاق العقاب وجب أن يزول استحقاق الثواب . وهذا هو العمدة الكبرى لهم في إثبات القول بالإحباط إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو نقل الفاسق من النار إلى الجنة، لكان دخول الجنة . إما أن يكون بالاستحقاق أو بالتفضل والأول باطل. لما بينا أن هذا الاستحقاق غير حاصل. والثاني أيضًا باطل لأن الجنة دار الثواب، لا دار التفضل . والدليل عليه : قوله تعالى : ﴿قَدْ أَفْلَحَ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ الَّذِينَ هُمْ فِي صَلَاتِهِمْ خَشِعُونَ وَالَّذِينَ هُمْ عَنِ اللَّغْوِ مُعْرِضُونَ وَالَّذِينَ هُمْ لِلزَّكَاةِ فَاعِلُونَ وَالَّذِينَ هُمْ لِفُرُوجِهِمْ حَافِظُونَ إِلَّا عَلَى أَزْوَاجِهِمْ أَوْ مَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَانُهُمْ فَإِنَّهُمْ غَيْرُ مَلُومِينَ فَمَنِ ابْتَغَىٰ وَرَاءَ ذَٰلِكَ فَأُولَٰئِكَ هُمُ الْعَادُونَ وَالَّذِينَ هُمْ لِأَمَانَاتِهِمْ وَعَهْدِهِمْ رَاعُونَ وَالَّذِينَ هُمْ عَلَىٰ صَلَوَاتِهِمْ يُحَافِظُونَ أُولَٰئِكَ هُمُ الْوَارِثُونَ الَّذِينَ يَرِثُونَ الْفِرْدَوْسَ﴾ [المؤمنون: ١-١١]. الشبهة الثانية: تمسكوا بالآيات المشتملة على لفظ الخلود في وعيد الفساد. كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَن يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِنًا مُتَعَمِّدًا فَجَزَاؤُهُ جَهَنَّمُ خَالِدًا فِيهَا﴾ [النساء: ٩٣] قالوا : الخلود هو الدوام. واحتجوا عليه بوجوه : أحدها قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا جَعَلْنَا لِبَشَرٍ مِّن قَبْلِكَ الْخُلْدَ﴾ [الأنبياء: ٣٤] ولا شك في أن من كان قبله قد جعل الله لهم لبثا طويلاً في الدنيا . فلو كان الخلود عبارة عن اللبث الطويل فقط لم يكن لهذه الآية معنى. وثانيها : إنه يصح تأكيده بلفظ التأييد قال تعالى : ﴿خَالِدِينَ فِيهَا أَبَدًا﴾ [النساء: ٥٧] ونص أهل اللغة على أن قوله «أبدًا» تأكيد لمعنى الخلود. فلولا أن لفظ الخلود يفيد الدوام، وإلا لم يصح تأكيده بما يفيد الدوام. وثالثها : إنه يصح أن يستثنى من الخلود أي مقدار أريد. فيقال : أنتم خالدون إلا كذا وكذا . والدليل عليه قوله : ﴿خَالِدِينَ فِيهَا مَا دَامَتِ السَّمَاوَاتُ وَالْأَرْضُ إِلَّا مَا شَاءَ رَبُّكَ﴾ [هود: ١٠٧]. الشبهة الثالثة: قوله تعالى في صفة الفجار : ﴿وَمَا هُمْ عَنْهَا بِغَائِبِينَ﴾ [الانفطار: ١٦] ولو خرجوا من النار لصاروا عنها غائبين . والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى من وجوه : الأول : إنها مبنية على أن المطيع يستحق بطاعته ثوابًا، والمذنب يستحق بذنبه عقابًا . ونحن لا نقول بذلك، فإنا لا نوجب على الله شيئًا.
As for the Muʿtazila, they have argued for eternal [punishment] by [several] points: The first doubt: that the sinner, by his sin, deserves punishment, and the deserving of punishment annuls what was previously established of deserving reward, because punishment is pure and lasting harm, and reward is pure and lasting benefit, and combining them is impossible, so combining their deservingness is impossible. And when the deserving of punishment is established, the deserving of reward must cease. And this is their major support in establishing the doctrine of iḥbāṭ. If this is established, we say: if the sinner were transferred from the Fire to Paradise, his entry into Paradise would either be by deserving or by favor. The first is false, because we have shown that this deserving does not occur. The second is also false, because Paradise is the abode of reward, not the abode of favor. The proof of that is His statement, exalted: 'Successful indeed are the believers, who are humble in their prayers, and who turn away from idle talk, and who are doers of zakah, and who guard their private parts except from their wives or those their right hands possess, for then they are not blameworthy, but whoever seeks beyond that, then those are the transgressors; and who are faithful to their trusts and their covenant, and who carefully maintain their prayers. These are the inheritors who will inherit Paradise.' (al-Mu'minun 1-11). The second doubt: They held to the verses that contain the word 'khulūd' in the threat against sin. Such as His statement: 'Whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding therein forever.' (al-Nisa 93). They said: 'khulūd' is perpetuity. They argued for this by [several] points: One of them is His statement: 'And We did not grant to any human before you eternity' (al-Anbiya 34). There is no doubt that those before him, God gave them a long stay in the world. So if 'khulūd' only meant a long stay, this verse would have no meaning. The second: that it is valid to emphasize it with the word 'ta'bid'. He said, exalted: 'abiding therein forever' (al-Nisa 57). And the scholars of language state that His word 'abadan' is an emphasis on the meaning of 'khulūd'. So if the word 'khulūd' did not indicate perpetuity, it would not be valid to emphasize it with what indicates perpetuity. The third: that it is valid to make an exception from 'khulūd' for any amount intended. So it is said: 'You are abiding except such and such.' The proof of that is His statement: 'Abiding therein as long as the heavens and the earth endure, except what your Lord wills.' (Hud 107). The third doubt: His statement, exalted, describing the wicked: 'And they are not absent from it' (al-Infitar 16). And if they left the Fire, they would become absent from it. The answer to the first doubt is by [several] aspects: The first: It is built upon the claim that the obedient person deserves reward for his obedience, and the sinner deserves punishment for his sin. And we do not say that, for we do not obligate anything upon God.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage presents three Mu'tazilite arguments for the eternal punishment of the grave sinner (fasiq) and begins the Ash'ari response to the first. The first argument is the major proof for iḥbāṭ: punishment is pure, lasting harm, and reward is pure, lasting benefit; they cannot be combined, so if punishment is deserved, reward must be annulled. From this, they argue that after punishment, the sinner cannot enter Paradise by either desert (since reward is gone) or divine favor (since Paradise is a place of reward, not favor), citing Qur'an 23:1-11. The second argument relies on verses using the word 'khulūd' (e.g., 4:93), claiming it means eternal duration. They support this with linguistic points: (a) the word is used for absolute eternity in 21:34, not just long stay; (b) it can be emphasized with 'abadan' (forever) in 4:57; (c) it allows exceptions, as in 11:107, showing it implies perpetuity unless specified. The third argument cites 82:16 that the wicked are 'not absent' from Hell, implying they never leave. Imam al-Razi then begins answering the first doubt. The first aspect of the response is to deny the premise that God is obligated to reward the obedient or punish the sinner. The Ash'ari position is that God does not owe anything to anyone; He may reward or punish as He wills. By rejecting human desert, the entire Mu'tazilite argument collapses. The answers to the second and third doubts are not provided in this passage.

Key Terms
خلود (khulūd)
تأبيد (ta'bīd)
استثناء (istithnā')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which verse do the Mu'tazila use to show that 'khulūd' means absolute eternity rather than a long stay?
A) Q. 4:93
B) Q. 21:34
C) Q. 4:57
D) Q. 11:107

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila used linguistic analysis of the word 'khulūd' and direct Qur'anic verses to argue that the punishment of the grave sinner is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to stand before Allah today, would you be confident that your faith and actions have secured your salvation, or is there a fear that you might fall short? The Mu'tazilites were so certain of their interpretation that they believed grave sinners would never be forgiven. How does your own certainty about your eternal fate compare to theirs, and what does that say about your reliance on Allah's mercy?
?
The Mu'tazila used linguistic precision to argue that 'khulūd' must mean forever. But when you read the Qur'anic threats, does your heart tremble at the possibility of eternal punishment, or do you quickly brush it aside with thoughts of God's mercy? How often do you allow the weight of these verses to shape your daily choices and repentance?
?
Consider the third doubt: the wicked are 'not absent' from Hell. If you examine your own life, are there sins you keep committing with the excuse that 'God will forgive me anyway'? How would your life change if you truly believed that you will never leave Hell if you die unrepentant? Let this lesson push you to a sincere repentance today.
